The reflexes that I discuss are the words for ‘day’ (§2.2.1), ‘water’ (§2.2.2), ‘udder’ (§2.2.3), ‘liver’ (§2.2.4), ‘excrement’ (§2.2.5), ‘spring’ (§2.2.6), ‘feather’ (§2.2.7), and ‘shelter, shed’ (§2.2.8).

### §2.2.1 THE WORD FOR ‘DAY’

The proto-form PG *dagaz [m., o-st.] ‘period from sunrise until sunset’ can be deduced from a set of cognates from all Germanic languages, e.g. Go. dags ‘ἡμέρα’, ON dagr, OSw. dagher, OHG tag, tac, OS dag, OFri. ī, dei, OE dæg, etc. all meaning ‘period from sunrise until sunset, day’. The word for ‘day’ has recently been connected by Kloekhorst (fthc.b, see particularly p.2, note 8) to a root *dh h- ‘to repeat itself (over and over again); a cycle’ that occurs in Lith. ã nas ‘many, often’, a nìn s ‘repeatedly’, a nùmas ‘frequency, multiplicitly’. This connection is very convincing and far more appealing than the received reconstruction to the root *dhegwh- ‘to burn’ (e.g. Orel, 2003:66). This connection is semantically not very fitting, for the sun may be said to burn, but not the day. In contrast, the semantic development from ‘to repeat itself (over and over again); cycle’ to the perpetually recurring ‘period from sunrise until sunset’ is very straightforward and attractive. Besides the form PG *dagaz, some forms occur with a long root vowel: Go. -dogs, ON œ and œ n, and OE ō o -. The ON variants œ and œ n have been claimed to reflect an old heteroclite paradigm and deserve a closer look. In ON we find a pair of formally and semantically very close forms, one with -r, the other with -n. This property is often taken to reflect ancient heteroclisy (already Benveniste, 1935:11). The forms with -r include: œ [n.] ‘half-day (night or day) of 12 hours’ (rarely also ’24-hour day’), OSw. dögher, ODan. døger ‘half-day of 12 hours’, also in ON t í œ a ‘two days’. The forms with -n include: œ n [n.] ‘half-day of 12 hours’, Norw. dialect døgn, dygn, Sw. dygn, Dan. døgn ’24-hour day’. We find a degenitival adverb in compounds vacillating between œ -is and œ n-is: ON sam œ s, sam œ n s, OSw. samdyghnis, Dan. (obsolete) samdøgn ‘within the same half-day’. Two remarkable properties of the forms are 1) a variant with -r and one with -n that are semantically virtually identical, 2) the lengthened grade in the root. In OE we find forms with the lengthened grade in ō o (GEN.SG

<!-- page 35 -->

ō o s) [n.] (also [m.]) meaning ‘day’, which either has r- or s-stem inflection.20 Go. -dogs (attested in the compounds NOM.SG ahtau-dogs ‘eight days, ὀκταήμερος’ in Ph 3:5, and

NOM.SG fidur-dogs ‘four days, τεταρταῖος’ in J 11:39) belongs here too.

In Indic (already R g-Veda) we find the r/n-stem áhar/ hnaḥ han(i) ‘day; today’, cf. Av. GEN.PL asnąm (to a stem azan-* ‘day’), besides an s-stem áhas- ‘day’. These forms have traditionally been connected to the Germanic forms and rightly so, in my opinion. The onset of the Indic r/n-stem áhar has been found to be problematic, however, and typically some kind of analogy or leveling is invoked. The form can be convincingly explained as an innerIndo-Iranian development, however. Starting from the root *dh h- ‘to repeat itself (over and over again); a cycle’, the r/n-stem formation is likely to have had a zero-grade root *dh h- in the paradigm. If this is true, then the *dh must have been lost before * h-. We can posit an original PD2 r/n-stem paradigm with the following development (as intimated by Puhvel, 1987:317, as well): 21

```text
PIE PIIr.1 PIIr.2 Vedic
N/A.SG *dhó h- > +dágh-ar → *ágh-ar > áh-ar
LOC.SG *dh h-én-i > +gh-án-i → *ágh-an-i > áh-an-i
```

The PIIr. transpose of *dhó h- was +dágh-ar and the weak stem yielded a PIIr. transpose +gh-án-i, so that the onsets +gh- and +dágh existed side by side at some point. Since this situation obscured the appurtenance of the forms to a single paradigm, a process of remedial analogy ensued. PIIr. took the strong stem +dágh- and leveled the absence of original *dh from the sequence *dh h- > +gh- in the weak stem, giving a new root that was leveled throughout the paradigm: *ágh- > Vedic áh-, Av. az-*. This effectively explains the Vedic and Avestan forms and points to an old PD1 r/n-stem *dhó h- /*dh h-én-. It is attractive to connect some of the Germanic forms, especially the Nordic forms œ, œ n and OE ō o -. There is one major phonological problem with this derivation and that is the length of the root vowel in the Germanic forms: the Germanic languages point to *ō, whereas the NOM.SG *dhó h- could not have lengthened the vowel (cf. the laws of lengthening proposed in §1.2, p.24). To resolve this issue we first have to examine the s-stems. In Vedic we also find an s-stem áhas- ‘day’. Mayrhofer (EWA I:154) considers the sstem form to be secondary, originating from the r/n-stem LOC.PL áhasu reinterpreted as áhassu. Not only is this interpretation forced and the LOC.SG not a very probable source for producing a new form, but the words for ‘yesterday’ may reflect an old s-stem *dh h-es- to the same root as the r/n-stem (of which the exact details are discussed below) anyway. That is, an s-stem probably already existed in PIE, which makes the strained explanation of Mayrhofer unnessecary. The s-stem áhas- can then readily be connected to the word for ‘yesterday’ *dh h-es-. The word may have lost its initial *dh along similar lines as the r/n-stem from a PD 20 For attestations see Darms (1978:179f.). The form in the Lindisfarne Codex <dœg> (and perhaps the late form ) may reflect / œ g/, a form with i-mutation of *ō indicating a proto-form * ō -ez. 21 I reconstruct R(ó), because the Lithuanian formation ã nas ‘many, often’ can be derived from the heteroclite as a thematization of virtual *dhó h-n-. This would point to a PD2 strong stem with R(ó). The leveling in Indo-Iranian must then have occurred after the working of Brugmann’s Law, for otherwise Brugmann’s Law could have applied in the open syllables of the leveled forms if the proposed sequence of developments occurred early enough. This remains tentative, however.

<!-- page 36 -->

stem *dhé h-s/*dh h-és-, although early analogy to the r/n-stem cannot be excluded (when the forms were still transparently connected to the same root). Moreover, if the root vocalism was original we would have expected a long vowel in the root instead, for the phonotaxis of the strong stem *CEC-s must have yielded a long vowel in this root by the law of vowel lengthening before contiguous final resonant (as described in §1.2, p.24). That is, we would expect a strong stem with a long root vowel, which in light of the long vowel in the Germanic forms must have been *ō: *dh h-s. This means that we would expect Vedic * has- << *dh h-s, which we don’t find. The Indic s-stem áhas must therefore have taken its root vocalism analogically from the r/n-stem. The Indo-Iranian material, then, shows reflexes of an inherited PD2 r/n-stem *dhó h- /*dh h-én- and marginally as well as of a PD2 s-stem

(*dh h-s/)*dh h-és-.

The two ON variants œ and œ n can hardly be explained as anything other than the reflexes of an old r/n-stem. However, the Germanic long vowel, which also occurs in Go. -dogs and OE ō o, is problematic. As indicated above, it is possible that the long vowel originated in the strong s-stem form *dh h-s. The OE form ō o can be interpreted as either an s- or r-stem and is ambiguous in its inflection. Nevertheless, since all extant r-stems are kinship terms (and r/n-stems were otherwise thematized) it is not likely that we are dealing with an r-stem, but rather with an s-stem instead. The mutation in the Northumbrian OE form œ (if this truly reflects a mutated long *ō) can be explained from either the PD2 weak suffix *-ér- or *-és-, with S(e). It may have been the case that in PN an s-stem * ō -eR existed, which still survives in ON dœgr. This is attractive, because * ō -eR (with strong R(ō) and weak S(e) corresponding to the typical s-stem N/A.SG suffix grade -S(e)- in Germanic) yields a transpose ON +dœgr which corresponds entirely to the attested dœgr. It seems to me that at some point in the development of ON the historical s-stem dœgr was fused with the r/n-stem, creating the two lexemes dœgr and dœgn.22 Further evidence for the existence of an s-stem in Germanic can be found in the Gothic personal name *Dagisþius (deduced from Δαγισθαῖος), showing an s-stem reflex that took the vowel from the ubiquitous o-stem formation PG *dagaz (Go. dags). This connection makes it at least possible that Go. -dogs (in auhtau-dogs and fidur-dogs) may have been an s-stem, perhaps directly reflecting the NOM.SG of the PD2 s-stem *dh h-s. It is remarkable that *dh h-s did not produce a regular s-stem Go. **dogis (cf. riqis ‘darkness’), but we may very well be dealing with an archaism. This is all the more likely since Go. dogs is not an r/n-stem, r-stem, or n-stem, but must be closely related to the ON and OE forms which probably contain an s-stem. Recently Kloekhorst (fthc.b) has argued that the IE words for ‘yesterday’ are related to the Germanic word for ‘day’.23 The weak PD2 stem *dh h-és- can account directly for Gr. 22 A remnant of the old r/n-stem is possibly retained in, OHG ta a ō, MDu. (e)raet, dageroit, MLG t, ēt, ōt, OE dægred, all ‘dawn’ < * a - -, with an undetermined suffix (with gradation?). ON a ‘opportune time’ is impossible to sever from this formation, but was folk-etymologized as a compound containing -ráð ‘counsel’. It is, then, possible to connect ON ǫ, ǫ verðr, OSw. daghwarþer (Västergotland daghur, with loss of final -ð) ‘breakfast’ as well (still containing the meaning of early day), which was folk-etymologized as a compound ǫ -verðr containing verðr ‘meal, food’. The form can tentatively be interpreted as stemming from *dagur-þ-. Perhaps the ON variation reflects alternants of a grading suffix, but it is unclear which suffix. Note that *dag(u)r- is exactly the shape that we would expect for Germanic from the heteroclite strong stem *dh h- as reconstructed above. 23 It is interesting that I arrive at some very similar conclusions as van den Oever (apud Kloekhorst), and it appears that our accounts complement each other nicely. Although the extent of van den Oever’s account

<!-- page 37 -->

χθες ‘yesterday’, for Alb. dje ‘yesterday’ < * s < GEN.SG *dh h-és(-s), for Lat. heri ‘yesterday’ < *dh h-és-i (DAT.SG, but probably used as a locative; the form is also continued in hesternus ‘of yesterday’), and for OIr. in-dé, MW doe, OCorn. doy ‘yesterday’ < *dh h-és(-i).24 Germanic *gestra- ‘yesterday’ belongs here too and also derives from the weak stem *dh h-és-. The form *ges-tra- is attested all over the Germanic world, e.g. in Go. gistradagis ‘tomorrow’, OHG gesteron, OE geostran, giestrandæg, MDu. gestern, all ‘yesterday’. The cognate form with enigmatic long vowel in Nordic forms such as ON í gær ‘yesterday’ must somehow be related as well. Now that the general derivation is outlined I discuss the forms in mre detail. The Nordic forms with a long vowel ON í r, í gjár, OSw., Sw. i går, Icel. , etc. ‘yesterday’ (the form occurs twice in Hamðismál, once meaning ‘yesterday’ in verse 22, once meaning ‘tomorrow’ in verse 306). The form has a cognate in the OE adverb a ‘(days of) yore’, also in compounds: -dagas ‘days of yore’, ær- a ‘heretofore; of old’. The form is usually interpreted as having a GEN.PL -a (thus Campbell, 1959:276), but this would be an isolated case form for an adverb. Rather, it attaches -a (originally < ‘ever’) used in temporal adverbs, cf. sōna ‘soon’. Hoffmann (1969) proposes that the form may derive from a proto-form containing *-sar as supposedly in ON vár ‘springtime’ < *wesar by some kind of contraction, but this solution is not compelling. Instead, I follow Gąsiorowski’s (2012) derivation and derive the long grade forms not from * ē1z but from * ē1r- < *gezr-' by Gąsiorowski’s Law by which *-Vsr-' > *-Vzr- (by Verner’s Law) > *- -. The form *gestracan be connected to this form from *dh h-és-ro- > *gestra with initial accent and the expected intrusive *-t- between (non-Verner) *-sr-. In this way we can unite the forms going back to *gestra- with the forms with long vowel in ON and OE, which is of course very attractive (although it would then separate the formation from the Latin adjective hesternus ‘of yesterday’ < *dh h-és- + -tr-). However, the reconstruction * ē1r- < *gezr-' as Gąsiorowksi proposes is not without difficulties. The vocalism of ON í r, which shows i- or R-mutation, requires an explanation. The form í gær may very well have been interpreted at some point as if it reflected original *R and mechanically got mutation of the vowel. OSw. continues the direct reflex of * ē1r< *gezr-', so that here the absence of mutation can readily be explained. The form gjár is explained by Hellquist as a later development from gær (Hellquist, 1922), which may very well have been dialectal (and is the usual form in compounds, cf. gjárdagr ‘yesterday’s day’, gjárkveld ‘yesterday’s night’). We then arrive at a derivation that not only connects the many forms of the word for ‘yesterday’ in different IE dialects with a Germanic word for ‘day’, but can explain an otherwise enigmatic set of forms (ON í gær, etc.) as well, where *gestra- < *dh h-és-ro- and * ē1r- < *gezr-' < *dh h-es-ró-. Finally, a word is in order about PG *dagaz ‘period from sunrise to sunset’ (Go. dags ‘day, ἡμέρα’, ON dagr, ODu. dag, OHG tag, OS dag, OFri. dei, dî, OE dæg, etc., all meaning ‘day, period from sunrise to sunset’). The form is not a thematized heteroclite, nor an s-stem, remains unknown to me, I believe that at least the reconstruction of a PD2 paradigm *dh h-s/*dh h-és- with long vowel in the strong stem *dh h-s by monosyllabic lengthening is a novel contribution. 24 The IIr. forms Skt. hyaḥ, Av. zyō ‘yesterday’ are best explained otherwise. Their alleged affinity to our word has barred a full understanding of the forms. The have been explained by van den Oever (apud Kloekhorst fthc.b:3) as resulting from *sm - s a compound meaning ‘the day adjacent to (this) day’, although Guus Kroonen (p.c.) suggests analogy to sadyas < *sm - s.

<!-- page 38 -->

and is not derived from these formations (we would moreover expect a neuter, not a masculine formation). It is therefore hard to reject the traditional connection of PG *dagaz to Skt. ni- h ḥ ‘heat, summer’ (cf. ha- ‘heat’) and Av. daz, Lith. dãgas, dagà ‘time of harvest, summer heat’, going back to *dhogwh-ós [m., o-st.] from a root *dhegwh- ‘treat with fire, burn’ (LIV2:133f.). That is, the formation may not at all derive from the same root as ON œ, œ n, Go. -dogs, and OE ō o. Originally the formation *dhogwh-ós may have been a τομός-formation (judging by the accent of Skt. ni- h - ‘heat, summer’), meaning something like ‘burning’, although an abstract τóμoς-formation ‘heat’ is more fitting. PG *dagaz must have been influenced semantically by the inherited words for ‘day’ (discussed above) in the course of development of Germanic when the roots became homophonous. The meaning ‘summer heat’ could have been used to refer to the heat of the day by association to the s- and r/n-stems for ‘day’, perhaps then developing via ‘(hot) summer’s day(time)’ to a more general and abstract meaning: ‘day, i.e. period from sunrise to sunset’. In conclusion, I’ve argued that we find evidence for ancient heteroclisy in ON dœgr and dœgn corresponding to the Indic heteroclite ha hnaḥ han(i), although the ON form must have been heavily influenced by the old s-stem. We can posit a PG s-stem *d -(e)z- as well as an ossified weak stem *dh h-és- in the word for ‘yesterday’, pointing to a PIE neuter PD2 s-stem. This yields the following two paradigms for PIE:

```text
s-stem r/n-stem
N/A.SG *dh h-s *dhó h-
GEN.SG *dh h-és-s *dh h-ér-s
```

This paradigm effectively unites a substantial number of IE forms for ‘day’ and ‘yesterday’ in a single paradigm. The Germanic material is especially interesting, for it retains both the strong stem dh h-s that was otherwise lost in the IE languages as well as the weak stem. Finally, I believe that PG *dagaz ‘period from sunrise to sunset’ derives from a different root.

### §2.2.2 THE WORD FOR ‘WATER’

In Gothic we find the form wato [n, n-st.] ‘water’ (GEN.SG watins, DAT.SG watin). The

```text
DAT.PL watnam (M 8,32, L 8,25) deviates from the regular pattern (which is -am, cf. hairtam
```

‘of/to the hearts’) and parallels the plural forms of the neuter namo ‘name’, an inherited nstem. OHG has uuazzar [n., o-st.] ‘water, lake, river’ and MHG wazzer, cf. the Cimbrian dialects of Siben Komoin (SCHM) bazzar and Lusern (BACH) wasɒr ‘water’, which retained the vowel timbre in the final syllable, but was lost elsewhere, e.g. in the Bavarian dialects of Laurein (KOLL) bͻsər [n.], Gottschee (TSCH) bossər, etc. ODu. has uuatar (WPs NOM.SG <uuatar>, NOM.PL <uuathir>, cf. the DAT.SG <uuatare> in CG, 891-900 AD). OS has the wellattested noun uuatar, uuater [n., o-st.] ‘water’. The OE form (Anglian and Mercian) wæter [n, o-st.] ‘water’ is very well attested, showing regular Anglo-Frisian brightening *a > æ in

<!-- page 39 -->

closed syllable, indicative of a thematic neuter form PG *u̯atr-aⁿ. ME water should have had a long vowel by ME open syllable lengthening, which would subsequently have been raised to /wētər/, cf. OE æcer > ModE. acre (= /ēkər/). However, trisyllabic shortening applied in some case forms which may have caused shortening or some case forms with a closed syllable would have prohibited lengthening, e.g. the GEN.SG watres. Interestingly, some modern English dialects sometimes do continue a lengthened, raised vowel ē, cf. Northumberland (WRIG) wētər, Lancashire (WRIG) wētə(r), wē ə(r), Cheshire (WRIG) wētə(r), etc. OFri. most frequently has weter [n, o-st.]. Besides forms with Frisian brightening, the form water occurs without any apparent pattern in the geographical distribution, a situation reflected in the modern dialects, cf. the ‘unbrightened’ forms in e.g. Schiermonnikoog (SPEN) våtər [n.], (HALB) wâtter, Mod.NFri. (LÖFS) wāa (although wetter occurs as well), Wangeroog and Brokmerland (REMM) watter, Saterlandic (REMM) Woater, and ‘brightened’ forms in e.g. Mod.WFri. and Hindeloopens (KOOY) wetter, and the pastor Johannes Cadovius Müller (1691) has the Harlingerland form wetter as well. In ON we find vatn [n., o-st] ‘water’, cf. Icel. vatn, Far. vatn, etc. Gíslason (1892:206f.) notes that in some texts vatr occurs where other manuscripts have vatn, cf.

```text
hvatt iordanr vatne (in Hrokkinskinna)
hvatr jordanr vatne (in Jöfraskinna)
hvatr iordanar ƿatri (in Fríssbók)
hvatr jordánar vatri (in Kringla)
hvatr-NOM.SG jordánr-NOM.SG vatr/ni-DAT.SG
‘(the) fast Jordan (for/to the) water’
```

The passage indicates that we are dealing with a noun and we can therefore exclude the possibility that the adjective vátr was used. In ON we thus find evidence for vatn and vatr, reflecting old heteroclisy.25 The name of the Swedish lake Vättern (with the definite clitic -n attached) is attested as Wetur (1222-1230 AD), Uætor (1231-1240 AD), Wætor (1289, 1297, 1454 AD), etc. (see Schalin:2010). Schalin wants to see a reflection of a holokinetic collective PIE *u̯ed-ōr in this form. However, I believe that the collective was *ud-ōr instead (cf. Gr. ὕδωρ), which is problematic for Schalin’s account. The form may be an archaism, however, and derive from the non-thematized strong PD2 stem *u̯ód-r as follows:

PIE *u̯ód-r > PG +u̯at-ur > PN *u̯atur + -R >> OSw. Vætur

The transpose of *u̯ód-r was ossified in PG +u̯at-ur (with vocalized +-ur < *-r). In PN the

NOM.SG -R ending was added analogically, which preserved the vowel of the final syllable.

The development is formally paralleled in the r-stem GEN.SG: fǫ < *faður-R (the ON form here took AD *- -s to an originally HD2 root), cf. already Boer (1920:145). The root vowel does not show u-mutation because it was taken analogically from OSw. vætn. Thus, in the 25 OSw. has vatn as well as vætn with analogical æ. It is hard to establish the source of analogy, but it is characteristic of OSw. a-stems to show variation between a and æ; one could think of spreading from the umlaut in the dative, but also note that the inflection of a- and ja-stems (with regular i-mutation) largely fell together in OSw., which is a more likely source of the widespread analogical mutation in my opinion (cf. also Noreen, 1904:291f., on the vacillation of a:æ in the a-stems).

<!-- page 40 -->

Nordic speech area we find evidence for old heteroclisy in the variants ON vatr : vatn, as well as an ossified strong stem *u̯ód-r in the OSw. lake name Vætur. Among the other Indo-European languages, Vedic, Greek, and Hittite provide the best evidence for the heteroclite stem.26 However, only Hittite continues the Proto-Indo-European heteroclite noun for ‘water’ intact. Remnants are found in other languages as well, providing additional evidence. In Hittite we find the NOM/ACC.SG (Old Script) u̯a-a-tar, u̯a-tar, which must go back directly to a strong stem *u̯ód-r with R(ó) and must reflect a PD2 paradigm. The weak stem is attested in e.g. the GEN.SG ú-u̯i5-te-na-aš and ALL.SG ú-e-te-na, etc. Kloekhorst (HIL:1142f. and fthc.a) convincingly argues that the Hittite forms with orthographically alternating e ~ i represent a reduced vowel /ə/ and not a full vowel /e/ or /i/. For instance, the GEN.SG ú-u̯i5-te-na-aš should be interpreted as /u̯adénaš/ with a reduced vowel inserted into the originally zero-grade root. The Hittite weak stem /u̯adén-/ must then reflect R(∅)-S(é) and go back to the weak PD form *udén-. This resolves the problematic traditional reading of the form as /u̯edenaš/ with two full vowels in the stem (as does Schindler, 1975). The Hittite NOM/ACC.PL is attested as ú-i-ta-a-ar (Old Script) and ú-e-da-ar (Old Script) which must reflect /u̯ədár/ with /ə/ for vacillating e ~ i in the spelling. The form /u̯ədár/ goes back directly to the collective HD3 strong stem *ud-ṓr which has an exact cognate in Greek ὕδωρ. This means that Hittite provides conclusive evidence for a PD2 singular stem *u̯ód-r/*ud-én- in addition to an HD3 collective strong stem *ud-. Schmidt (1889:195) showed that the Greek form ὕδωρ ‘water’, GEN.SG ὕδατος, was actually an old collective (cf. also Schindler, 1975a). Functioning grammatically as a singular, the collective came to be used for the singular in Greek. The formal correspondence with the ancestor of Hittite N/A.PL ú-i-ta-a-ar (= / t /) is striking as both the Greek and Hittite forms must go back to the PIE strong collective stem *ud-ṓr. This invariably points to a gradation of the strong form as R(∅)-S(ō) of the HD3 paradigm. Vedic Sanskrit has some rather interesting forms which have proved resistant to a satisfactory interpretation. The following forms are attested in Vedic:

```text
NOM.SG udakám, ud (RV),
GEN.SG udnáḥ
LOC.SG udán, udáni
N/A.PL udā́
INS.PL udabhis
```

The NOM.SG *udakám* is a *ka*-derivation to the stem *ud-n̥-. A derived form for the strong cases can hardly have been part of the original PIE heteroclite paradigm. A heteroclite strong stem is furthermore unlikely to have contained the weak suffix *-n-. The form udakám must be an innovation that has a parallel in pāvakaḥ ‘fire’ < virtual *peh2 n - (cf. §3.2.1.3, p.75) and 26 In Slavic we find cognate forms such as OCS voda, Ru. vodá, SCr. vòda ‘water’, probably from a proto-form containing a consonant cluster *-ndn- from earlier *-dn- with subsequent simplification to *-nd- (Kortlandt, 1979:60f.). In Lith. we find vanduõ (dial. unduõ) ‘water’ and OPruss. wundan ‘water’ from * on - < * o -n-. If the form * o -n- actually was the precursor to the Balto-Slavic material it would continue the strong PD2 gradation R(ó)-E(∅), but with leveling of the weak suffix *-n- instead of original *- (quite similar to ON vatn). Latin unda ‘wave’ may be interpreted similarly, so that: *udn- > *undn- > *und- (cf. also EDLIL:641). It is in principle possible that *ud-n- reflects the weak collective stem.

<!-- page 41 -->

entirely replaces a reflex of *u̯ód-r. Another NOM.SG is attested in the RV: ud, which can hardly be original either as it has neither a gradation vowel, nor a heteroclite strong stem

suffix *-r-.

Recently, Lubotsky (2013) has argued that the N/A.SG udakám was not actually the strong stem to the weak stem udán-, but that instead the word provided the strong stem to weak udán-. Lubotsky argues that there is a minute but important semantic difference between udaká-, used mostly for ‘water (for drinking)’, and the collective meaning of udán-. However, the fact that the word udaká- ‘water’ often refers to drinking water is hardly surprising. Moreover, some examples seem strained and hardly refer to ‘water (for drinking)’ more than ‘water (as a collective)’, e.g. (p.161): S 4.16.3cd utó samudraú váru asya kuks, ut smínn álpa udaké níl naḥ “also the two oceans are Varu a’s paunches; also in this petty water is he hidden.” It is therefore very difficult to contrast the meaning of udaká- with the udán- ‘water (as a collective)’ and to suppose that rather than udaká- provides the strong stem to the weak stem udán-. In addition, Lubotsky provides examples of similar sentences where is used as alternatively to udán-, but since both words mean ‘water’ this is not at all surprising. Etymologically the word N/A.SG is connected to e.g. OIr. fír ‘milk’ and MW gwir-awt ‘strong drink, liquor’, a thematization of * h1r-, OE w ‘liquid’ and ON vári ‘liquid, blood’ are also formed to * h1r-, and e.g. OPrus. wurs ‘pond’ from the zero-grade < PIE *uh1r- (cf. NIL:715ff.). Lubotsky proposes that can be connected to * - by a sound law *h1 < *d, working in e.g. Gk. ἑκατόν ‘100’ < *h1k m t m < *dk m t m. The sound law is not widely accepted, however, and Lubotsky admits that the environment is not the same as in the adduced forms, which is not persuasive. But there is a more pressing problem. Since the form is connected to e.g. ON vári ‘liquid, blood’ and OIr. fír ‘milk’ < * h1r-, which unambiguously point to R(é) as opposed to R(ó) in * -, the derivation of < * - must be rejected. In contrast to Lubotsky’s claims, it seems to me quite evident that there may have been several forms in Vedic replacing the original strong stem * ó -, namely: udaká-, , and ud. In any way, there is no reason to derive from * -. The GEN.SG udnáḥ is not reliable evidence against a PD paradigm, because Sanskrit leveled HD -nas everywhere, always replacing PD +-ans (cf. §1.1.4, p.22). The only reliable singular forms are LOC.SG udán and udáni. These locative forms provide evidence for a weak PD stem *ud-én-. The NOM/ACC.PL corresponds entirely to Hittite ú-i-ta-a-ar (= / ə /), Greek ὕδωρ, and to which we can also add Umbr. utur, which all go back to an HD3

NOM/ACC.COL *ud-ṓr. An INS.PL udabhis is also attested, which reflects *ud-n̥-bhi(-s),

showing a weak HD3 stem *ud-n-'. The Vedic material, then, provides evidence for a weak PD stem *ud-én- in the singular, although there is no evidence for a strong PD stem. Compelling evidence for the HD3 collective *ud-ṓr/ud-n- is found as well. On the basis of the evidence of Hittite, Greek, and Vedic I posit a PD2 singular and a HD3 collective for PIE:

<!-- page 42 -->

```text
SINGULAR COLLECTIVE
NOM.SG *u̯ód-r *ud-ṓr
GEN.SG *ud-én-s *ud-n-ós
```

The West-Germanic forms point to PG *u̯at-r-aⁿ and the ON vatr both point to *u̯at-r-aⁿ, which must continue a thematization of the strong stem *u̯ód-r. A virtual *wat-n-aⁿ deduced from Nordic forms must have taken the *-n- from the weak stem. The OSw. lake name Vætur continues the non-thematized strong stem *u̯ód-r directly (although at a later point in its development it must have added NOM.SG -R in order to retain the final syllable vowel intact in OSw.). Gothic wato is an n-stem, which means that it was absorbed by the neuter n-stems.

### §2.2.3 THE WORD FOR ‘UDDER’

The word for ‘udder’ is continued as a heteroclite in Vedic ū dhar (GEN.SG ū hnaḥ) as well as in Greek οὖθαρ (GEN.SG οὔθατος). Latin has ūber ‘breast, udder’, which is explained by De Vaan (EDLIL:636) as follows: *HuHdh-r would have resulted in PITal *waþ-, but *Hóu̯Hdh-r could yield ūber by causing early loss of the second laryngeal. This makes it likely that Greek had o-grade * h- as well (instead of assuming vocalization of laryngeal in *#h3u-), whereas Indic then continues the zero-grade form *HūHdh-. Recently, Vine (2010) has connected the word for ‘udder’ with a root *h1eu̯h1dh- reconstructed for the Russian (southern Vyatka) forms 3PL udut ‘they are ripening’, Novosibirsk t’ ‘to ripen (of grain), to fill/swell up with liquid’. This is in principle possible, although the forms are isolated and could perhaps be derived differently. Nevertheless, this is the most attractive theory so far. The alternation of R(o: ∅) in the root indicates an originally PD2 paradigm:

```text
NOM.SG *h1óu̯h1dh-r
GEN.SG *h1uh1dh-én-s
LOC.SG *h1uh1dh-én-i
```

A PG proto-form *ūdra- can be deduced from e.g. OS ūder, MDu. (Kiliaan, 1339) uder, uyder, huyder, wder, wr, ore, (Plant., 1515, has uder oft udder), OHG ūta, ūta o, OFri. ūder, OE ūder, all meaning ‘udder’. OHG reflects two related variant forms for ‘udder’. The first is ūta, ūta o (cf. Cimbrian (SCHM) Autar < *ūta, High Alemannic of Kanton Schaffhausen (Wann) ūttər, etc.), a form with an anaptyctic -a- from WG *ūdr < *ūdr-aⁿ. Secondly, the OHG form ūtiro is attested, with -ir-. The high vowel in -ir- can explain the i-mutation in MHG iuter and HG Euter ‘udder’. The OHG variation -ar: -ir- points to an original suffix gradation S(∅: e), where *e > i occurred in *-ér-i > PG *-ir-i. In OS we find geder (Oxford Virgil glosses), MLG has jeder (cf. Göttingen dialect (SCHA) gidder, Bremen dialect (TILI) Jidder, Gidder, Holstein dialect (SCHÜ) Jidder, Jüdder, Jödder, Gronings (BOEL) Jâr, etc.) and OFri. has (cf. WFri. (WFT) jaar, also ûr, EFri.

<!-- page 43 -->

(STÜR) Jüdder, Jadder, Jidder, Schiermonnikoog (SPEN) iudər, Wangeroog (Faltings, 1983:209) jedder, etc. all ‘udder’). According to NEW these forms go back to *eu̯dura-. However, I would instead posit *eu̯dra-. In LG we find that * a- lowers the diphthong by the following *-a- yielding *ieder. In the Vergil glosses we find the corresponding geder < * < *ieder. In Low Saxon dialects, e.g. older Overijssel (1836, HALB) gier, we find a contraction from geder. In Frisian we find that the proto-form * a- gave * > j. There are some inner-dialectal differences in the development of North Frisian, because of the irregular reflexes of vowels. That is, the form * dr- > * ēdr-, with gemination of *dr > *ddr, further raising to *ī with shortening *ī > *i before a cluster: * -. In Wangeroog jedder the short vowel e is unexpected. It may have originated as follows according to Faltings (1983:209ff.): * - > * ē - with earlier shortening before *dr and then to *jedder- with gemination. Schiermonnikoog iudər and WFri. ûr go back to *ū - and thus belong to the zero-grade root *ū a-. It is interesting to find that two forms existed side by side in these languages, one going back to *ū a- and the other to * a-. This variation reflects older living root gradation R(e: ∅). We can furthermore connect ON júgr, júr ‘udder’ < * (with *-ur-, because *-er- would have caused lowering of the diphthong), where *-g- also developed from PN *-ð- in f ǫ [n.] ‘four’ < *fjæður < *kw t (ō)r. The variation * a-: * a- points to a PG * -ur < *h1 h- besides an early thematization PG * -r-awhich prohibited the vocalization of the liquid *-. Taken together, the root gradation R(e: ∅) and suffix gradation S(∅: e) implies a neuter PD1 paradigm: * -(u)r-/*ū -er- < *h1 h1dh- /*h1uh1dh-én-s. It is quite remarkable that we find a PD1 form in Germanic, but require a PD2 form for Latin and Greek.

### §2.2.4 THE WORD FOR ‘LIVER’

An Indo-European heteroclite stem for ‘liver’ can be secured from evidence of most Indo-European languages. Although this word alone requires book-length treatment, for it is probably the most discussed heteroclite in Indo-European linguistics, a concise treatment of this important word is in order. I believe that the appurtenance of the Germanic forms for the word for ‘liver’ can be proved and I shall start here by discussing the Germanic material first. In the Nordic area we find the word ON lifr (stem lifr-, cf. GEN.SG lifrar), Icel. lifur, OSw. liver, Sw. lever, etc. all with the sense of ‘liver’. In OHG we find libera, lepera, leuera, lebra etc. [f.] ‘liver’, (typically still trisyllabic in the Cimbrian and Alemannic dialects, cf. Cimbrian (SCHM) lebara, lebera, in Highest Alemannic e.g. Kanton Glarus (WINT) l b &, in High Alemannic dialects e.g. Kanton Schaffhausen (WANN) lεbərə). Furthermore, OE has lifere, lifre, OFri. has livere (EFri. of Johannes Cadovius Müller (1691, KÖNI) has leffer, Söl’ring dialect (MÖLL) liwer) and MLG has lever, ‘liver’. Philippa (EWN) and Orël (2003) deduce a proto-form *lib(a) ō- from the material. The *a in parentheses in this reconstruction is surely based on the OHG evidence, which vacillates between spelling a and e. It is characteristic of OHG to spell a for an anaptyctic vowel (cf. the spelling of the word without anaptyxis e.g.

<!-- page 44 -->

lebra, lebre), although the anaptyctic vowel e may have assimilated to the root vocalism (cf. BRRE15:64ff. on anaptyxis). On the other hand e might as well be an original vowel, but neither the originality nor anaptyctic origin of e can be determined with certainty. However, the remarkable variation in Cimbrian (SCHM) lebara, lebera suggests that original suffix variation may have survived. Hence, I tentatively deduce a proto-form *lib(e)r-ō from the

Germanic material.

Sanskrit has a NOM.SG yak -t ‘liver’ with R(é) in the absence of Brugmann’s Law. The

```text
GEN/ABL.SG yaknaḥ cannot be trusted (cf. §1.1.4, p.22), but the LOC.SG yakani (AV) indicates
```

a weak PD stem with S(é). De Vaan (2003:68f.) explains that the hapax y ka ə must be yakarə with a short root vowel (proved by Persian jigar, Khotan. gyagarra < *yak na- as well as Sanskrit yak -t). The Indo-Iranian forms thus reflect an originally PD1 paradigm: * kw- / ikw-én-. Ancient Greek has the heteroclite form ἧπαρ/ἥπατος ‘liver’ with long vowel initially. Szemerényi (1975:333) explains that the long vowel is not original, but the form took the long vowel analogically from the semantically close forms ἦτορ ‘heart’, ἦτρον ‘abdomen’, or κῆρ ‘heart’. The meaning of ‘fish roe’ in both OCS ikra ‘fish roe’ and OIr. iuchair ‘fish roe, caviar’ may point to a collective. OIr. iuchair ‘fish roe, caviar’ can be derived from *ikwōri-, which is likely to be an i-stem derivation of the strong collective (HD3) stem *ikwōr. OCS ikra ‘fish roe’ can be derived from a double zero-grade strong stem *ikwr-. We may assume leveling of weak R(∅) in the strong stem. These forms all corroborate a heteroclite PD1 reconstruction * kw- /*ikw-én- with a corresponding HD3 collective. Forms with initial *l appear for instance in OPr. lagno glossing Leber ‘liver ’(a hapax in the Elbing Vocabulary), which does not have to be emended to *jagno. The R(ó) finds a parallel in Latin, and may reflect confusion with the PD2 paradigm. In Armenian we find another cognate with initial *l: leard ‘liver’, GEN.SG lerdi, which can be derived from *l kwr-. From this survey, a PD1 paradigm * kw- /*ikw-én- can be deduced with certainty (neglecting the marginal evidence for a PD2 paradigm, cf. below on Latin). Besides the Germanic forms, Old Prussian and Armenian provide evidence for initial *l- or *l - which was lost in most languages, suggesting an original heteroclite paradigm *l kw- /*likw-én-. The word for ‘liver’ in classical Latin is iecur with R(e), also in GEN.SG iecoris (later iecinoris), but there are also forms with R(o) such as iocur (Pliny), GEN.SG iocinoris, iocineris. These Latin forms in particular have led to many fanciful solutions for the PIE word for ‘liver’. Rix (1965) established that the oldest Latin paradigm for the word ‘liver’ was iecur/iociner-. In the absence of a convincing explanation by Latin sound laws for the alternation of R(e: o), Beekes (1985:4ff.) assumes an early, PIE darkening of *e that was inserted analogically into the weak stem: *ikw-én- → * kw-én- > * okw-én-. But this seems very unlikely, for even if this change was PIE, it seems to have ceased to work later on in PIE (cf. reconstructions as *s hesos, *bherete, etc., which Beekes also argues to be of later date), but then suddenly appears to work again in pre-Latin Indo-European. This seems very unlikely. If the form * okw-én- was truly old, on the other hand, R(o) would probably have been far more widespread. In any case, this PIE explanation is then meant to solely to account for a Latin form and is entirely ad hoc. Alternatively, the original PD1 gradation R(é) in the root may have been confused with PD2 R(ó), as occurring in for instance the heteroclite for ‘water’. That is, the very close correspondence between PD1 and PD2 and the fact that both gradation types occur in heteroclites may have caused such confusion in this word. This can

<!-- page 45 -->

potentially also explain the R(ó) in OPruss. lagno and Let. akna, akne, etc. It remains problematic, however, why forms such as iecinoris existed besides iocineris, where the vowels are metathesized. These patterns may have arisen secondarily in Latin as the earliest pattern was iecur/iociner- according to Rix.27 The paradigm iecur/iociner-, which shows R(e: o), could be explained by an AS paradigm (cf. Schindler, 1975), although this is not corroborated by other Indo-European languages. Likewise, the typical step of positing a variant locative stem with R(ó) is a step that requires particularly solid argumentation, for a locative stem that is different from both the strong and weak stem is not found in Latin, nor in any other language. Instead, I propose that Latin may have had influence from a PD2 paradigm, or even an alternative PD2 paradigm. Thence it is probable that the virtual PD2 strong stem * kw- is continued in the relic iocur in Pliny. The earliest Latin paradigm established by Rix, iecur/iociner-, must then go back to a PD1 *iecur/*i(e)ciner-, but was influenced by the PD2 variant. In any way, the Latin material aligns with the reconstruction of a PD1 paradigm, but the forms reflecting R(o) quite possibly reflect PD2 interference. Due to limitations of space and time, I cannot enter into a comprehensive discussion of the each and every independent proposal and solution that has been made in the literature. Nevertheless, at least a survey of theories is in order. Here follows a representative synopsis of recent proposals for the reconstruction of the PIE word for ‘liver’ (adapted from NIL:393,

```text
references therein):
NOM/ACC.SG GEN.SG LOC.SG
*ljēkert?? Schmidt (1889)
*l ēkw- -t?? Benveniste (1935)
* kw- *ikw-n-és * okw-én Rix (1965)
* kw- (-t) * kw-n-os? Eichner (1973)
* kw- * kw-n -s? Schindler (1975)
* kw- * kw-n -s * kw-r-i Normier (1980)
* h1kw- -t *ih1kw-n-ós *ih1kw-én Hamp (1983)
* kw- * okw-én-s (← *ikw-én-s) Beekes (1985)
* kw- * ókw-n -s * ekw-én Nussbaum (1998)
* kw- * kw-n -s * okw-én (← *ikw-én) Mottausch (2000)
* kw- *H kw-n-os *(H) okw-én Neri (2003)
* ókw- *H kw-n -s *(H) kw-n Tremblay (2004)
```

Many proposals for gradation patterns have been made simply to account for the word for ‘liver’. More specifically, the reconstructions typically attempt to account for the Latin forms of the word iecur in which unexplained o-grade occurs, which can more easily be explained as a Latin innovation. The proposals are often invoked to account for the Latin variation and are thus entirely ad hoc. What’s worse, the proposed gradation types often have no probative parallels anywhere else. As I explain in the §2.1.3, p.33, heteroclites are normally either PD or 27 De Vaan (EDLIL:296) points out a “phonetic ratio” where io- occurs in pretonic position, while the syllable that was stressed in pre-classical and classical times had ie-. This secondary development may perhaps have been a subconscious attempt to ‘make sense’ of the PD1: PD2 variation by the Roman Sprachgefühl.

<!-- page 46 -->

static, and a deviation from these patterns itself requires compelling legitimizing grounds. In fact, there is no compelling evidence for a separate locative stem with its own peculiar gradation pattern in any attested language and any proposal invoking such a special gradation pattern for the locative should be rejected. This observation in itself disqualifies most of the proposals given in the literature, namely Rix (1965), Normier (1980), Hamp (1983), Nussbaum (1998), Mottausch (2000), Neri (2003), and Tremblay (2004). We can reduce the sheer number of proposals further by positing some constraints which any reconstruction must satisfy. From the survey of the reconstructions for ‘liver’ above and from the forms I discussed above, we can derive some general constraints: 1) the form was a neuter heteroclite stem, 2) the strong stem had R(e), although there is evidence for secondary R(o) as well (Latin, Baltic), 3) no forms appear to have had a long root vowel (Greek and Avestan). This disqualifies the proposals of the proposals of Eichner (1973) and Schindler (1975). The only reconstruction that satisfies these constraints is the one of Beekes (1985), who also reconstructs a PD paradigm. We can add to this the observation that 4) three language groups (Germanic, Armenian, Old Prussian) have initial *l, which should therefore be taken seriously, but most others have *. Any account must explain these properties and these constraints immediately pose a problem for many theories. Finally, it is possible to connect the Germanic word for ‘liver’ to the Indo-European word for ‘liver’, as done already by Schmidt (1889:198f., cf. Benveniste, 1935:8f.). Schmidt (1889:198f.) already posited a root “*l ēkert”, which fits the material rather well. Updating Schmidt’s reconstruction, I then posit a PIE PD1 heteroclite paradigm *l kw- /*likw-én-:

```text
SINGULAR COLLECTIVE
NOM.SG *li̯ékw- *likw-
GEN.SG *likw-én-s *likw-n-ós
```

The unusual onset cluster *l̥i̯e- was simplified in most languages, typically losing *l-, or generalizing *l- or *i̯e-, or in the case of Germanic continuing the zero-grade root with retention of *li-. I will now discuss the details of the derivation of the Germanic form in more

detail.

The heteroclite weak PD1 stem *likw-én- would have either yielded PG +ligw-én- or +lib-én- (both forms have voicing by Verner’s Law and the latter form has the sporadic *kw > *f (or *b via Verner’s Law) as in e.g. * lfaz ‘wolf’ < * l kwos). The transpose +lib-én- is remarkably close to the deduced variant reconstruction *liber-ō-. Note that the required S(é), which is reconstructed in the gradation of the suffix S(∅: e) from the OHG forms, can explain at once the zero-grade root and the voicing from Verner’s Law. Furthermore, gradation S(∅: e) is expected in a PD1 paradigm. We can explain the reconstruction *lib-erby assuming the generalization of the strong suffix *-r- and the absorption of the stem into the Germanic ō-stems. The form may very well have been incorporated into the Germanic ōstems by analogy to a word that is formally and semantically very close: the τομή-formation PG *laib-ō ‘remains’ (Go. laiba, ON leif, OE l f, etc.) < *lo kw-éh2 to the root *l kw-, cf. Lith. lìkti ‘remain’ (cf. Bammesberger, 1990:106172). The roots *l kw- and *l kw- were formally virtually identical and the sense of ‘remains’ and ‘liver’ are semantically not far removed at all. This observation in itself further corroborates a reconstruction of our form as a

<!-- page 47 -->

reflex of *l kw-. The deduced proto-form *lib-r- with S(∅) would then reflect the strong suffix *- (which had not yet vocalized to *-ur) and indicate that at some point the weak root gradation was generalized in the paradigm, but suffix variation was still present. This points to an original PIE heteroclite PD1 paradigm *l kw- /*likw-én-, which developed into PG *lib-r-ō-/*lib-ér-ō (generalizing weak *lib- and strong *-r-, and was then absorbed into the ō-

stems).

### §2.2.5 THE WORD FOR ‘EXCREMENT’

A heteroclite word for ‘excrement’ is found in Greek σκῶρ/σκατός. This is the collective form *sk-ṓr (like ὕδωρ ‘water’ < *ud-ṓr, cf. §2.2.2, p.38) with the typical Greek intrusive t in the genitive σκατός < *skn̥tós from *sk-n-ós). In Hittite we find the corresponding heteroclite stem šākkar/šaknaš, where šākkar continues the strong N/A.SG *sók-r, but the genitive šaknaš causes some problems. I believe this is simply the direct reflex of the collective form *sk-n-ós (as the genitive singular would have yielded something like **šakenaš or **iškenaš). Av. sairiia- ‘dung’ Russ. sor ‘dung’, etc. are cognate and possibly Skt. ava-skara- [m.] ‘excrement’ and perhaps Lat. -(s)cerda (in e.g. muscerda and sucerda, cf. HIL:807f.). It is possible that the form is derived from the verbal root *sekH- ‘separate, cut off, distinguish’ (LIV2:524), meaning ‘that which is separated > that which is secreted > excrement’, but this cannot be proved. We can tentatively deduce a PD2 singular and HD3 collective as follows:

```text
SINGULAR COLLECTIVE
NOM.SG *sók(H)-r *sk(H)-
GEN.SG *sk(H)-én- *sk(H)-n-ós
```

In Germanic we find ON skarn, OE scearn, OFri. skern, MLG scharn, etc, meaning ‘dung, excrement’. The form PG *skarna- is reconstructed on the basis of these forms, which is often held to be related to the heteroclite *sók(H)- /*sk(H)-én-. However, the proto-form PG *skarna- cannot be straightforwardly connected. It may show contamination of the strong suffix *-r- and the weak *-n-, but this can hardly explain the occurrence of the vowel. Perhaps the form is based on the weak stem *skH-én- with contamination of the strong suffix *-r-, which would require *skh23-én- with *h23 to color the vowel. This would require a great number of assumptions, however. On the other hand we may be dealing with a *-nó- concrete derivation (Krahe & Meid, 1967:106) to the collective *sk - → *sk -ná- > *skar-na- (with shortening by Osthoff’s Law). The form remains unclear, but may very well be related to the heteroclite *sók(H)- /*sk(H)-én-.

<!-- page 48 -->

### §2.2.6 THE WORD FOR ‘SPRINGTIME’

An Indo-European heteroclite word for ‘spring’ or ‘springtime’ can be gathered from a large number of Indo-European languages: Latin has ē, Gr. ἔαρ < *ϝέσαρ ‘spring’, Skr. vasantá- ‘spring’ (from virtual * s-en-tó-), vasar- ‘morning’ (< * s- ), Av. LOC.SG aŋh - ‘spring’ < * s-r-i, Arm. garun (gar- < *gehar < * s- ), Russ. vesna ‘spring’, and in ON we find vár ‘spring’. The Latin long vowel in ē is explained by De Vaan (EDLIL:663f.) from a PIE paradigm * s- /* s-n-os which yielded (in my terms) the transpose +veror/+ ēnos, where the GEN.SG + ēnos took the *-r- and subsequently generalized the stem * ē -. It is unclear if Skr. vasantá- ‘spring’ reflects original S(é), which could point to a PD1 paradigm. However, the Avestan LOC.SG aŋh - ‘spring’ < * s-r-i and e.g. Russ. vesna ‘spring’ < * sn- all show zero-grade in the suffix. This S(∅) rather indicates static gradation with R(é) throughout the paradigm. The Latin scenario requires a static paradigm as well. De Vaan’s starting point is * s- /* s-n-os, with a weak stem * s-n-. Skr. vasantá- seems to have attached the suffix *-tó- to the stem * s-en-, but can perhaps better be explained as having restructured the stem with a suffix *-ont- or *-ent- and may then not reflect the weak heteroclite stem directly at all (or analogy to hemantá- ‘winter’, which is only first attested in AV, however). Furthermore, Lith. vãsara and Latv. vasara ‘summer’ must be closely related, but they show R(ó). It is possible that R(é: ó) alternated within the paradigm, which would imply acrostatic gradation. However, since there are no good examples of acrostatic gradation outside of the root nouns (as judged by Kloekhorst, 2013:108ff.), the isolated vowel grade of the Baltic forms may not be original. Perhaps the Baltic forms took their root vocalism in analogy to other heteroclites with R(ó) (although it remains unclear which one)? Regardless of the aberrant Baltic material, the otherwise consistent reflection of R(e)-S(∅) in all case forms, combined with the absence of evidence for a weak stem with R(∅)-S(é), in the Indo-European dialects suggests an original static paradigm. I therefore tentatively reconstruct the following static paradigm:

```text
NOM.SG * s-
GEN.SG * s-n-s
DAT.SG * s-n-i
```

Gąsiorowski (2012) connects the ON form vár (cf. OSw., Sw. vår, Norw. vår, Dan. Vaar, all ‘spring’) to the Indo-European word for ‘spring’ from the thematization of * s-, * s-ró-. The form * s-ró- yielded * z a- by Verner’s Law and then * ē1ra- by Gąsiorowski’s Law of loss of *z before *r with compensatory lengthening. Hofmann (1969) furthermore connects Frisian w s, wē s ‘spring’ (cf. NFri. (OUTZ) wors ‘spring’). To explain the final -s Hofmann proposes metathesis as in ON vár. However, it is not possible for a phoneme to metathesize when it was first lost with compensatory lengthening from * z a-.28 The Frisian word for spring is also attested in LG, e.g. in Gronings (BOEL) wâr, Gronings 28 Hofmann tries to back his claim by adducing metathesis from elsewhere in the Frisian language area, and compares Saterl.Fri. jérssene, Wangeroog jursen ‘yesterday’ (to which can be adduced an older form in Johannes Cadovius Müller (1691, KÖNI) yurssen meehn ‘yesterday morning’,). However, this form is not understood very well in the first place and obscurity cannot cast light on other obscure forms.

<!-- page 49 -->

dialect of Hunsego (MOLE) woars ‘spring’, etc. The fact that the form is limited to a Low German dialect with a Frisian substrate indicates that the form is probably Frisian in origin. Boelens has something interesting to say about the word that could explain the unexpected final -s. The word wâr is according to Boelens only used in fixed expressions with the genitive case. That is, the -s is actually a genitive ending, but the fixed collocations in the genitive case has led to the erroneous view that the -s was part of the stem. This means that the actual form could have been wâr. The exact origin of the vowel in the Frisian forms w s, wē s ‘spring’ is unclear according to Hofmann, and the vowel of the Gronings word wâr cannot help us much if it is a substrate word. It is in principle at least possible to connect the forms to ON vár, etc. The Gronings form wâr may (via Frisian) go back to WG *wār- < PG *wē1r-a- < earlier *wezr-a-, but this is extremely tentative considering the difficulties surrounding substrate words in Gronings.29

### §2.2.7 THE WORD FOR ‘FEATHER’

The word for ‘feather’ in Hittite patta pattanaš or p tta p ttanaš ‘wing, feather’. The word is everywhere spelled with the sign BAD, which is ambiguous. The sign may be interpreted as either pát- or pét-/pít- (Rieken, 1999:309, HIL:763). First of all, the geminate - tt- must be explained from *-t- plus a laryngeal (HIL:763). Since the plene spelled (Old Script) DAT/LOC.SG pád-da-a-ni (besides non-plene, e.g. pa-at-ta-ni) indicates a suffix -an-, the paradigm must have been PD (HD would have yielded a zero-grade suffix). This means that we must assume that the laryngeal was *h2, which would have colored the vowel in the PD suffix *-en- (again following HIL:763). However, the reconstruction of *h2 is problematic for some of the cognates. For example, Greek has πτερόν ‘wing, feather’ and Arm. tcer ‘leaf (of dough)’, both derive from < *pt-er-. Were the laryngeal *h2, then it would have colored the suffix vowel to *a. The Greek form πτερόν, for instance, would rather point to *pth1-ér/nwith *h1 than to *pth2-ér/n-, as otherwise we would have attested to **πταρόν. That is, πτερόν can be analyzed as πτ-ερ-όν with πτ-ερ- reflecting *pth1-én- with *h1 and analogical *-r- from the strong form. This is a pressing issue. Other languages continue some permutation of the original heteroclite alternation, cf. Lat. penna (< *petna-) ‘feather’. In Indo-Iranian we find the cognates Skt. pátram [n.] ‘wing’ besides pátvan- ‘flying’ (with unexpected -van-), patará‘winged, flying’, Av. patarə-ta ‘winged’, thus reflecting forms with *-r- as well as *-n-. The irreconcilable reconstruction of *h2 for Hittite and *h1 for Greek and Armenian may have been due to the confusion of very similar roots. As the LIV2 shows, there are three roots that are formally and semantically very close: *peth1- ‘to fall’ (LIV2:477f.), *peth2- ‘to fly’ (LIV2:479), and *peth2- ‘to spread out’ (LIV2:478f., which may have arisen under influence 29 In Föhr and Sylt the form Uurs is attested, specifically in the expressions Uurs, in Uursem, auwer Uurs ‘in spring’. The form Uurs goes back either to *ur- or *ū -. Is it possible that we are here dealing with an archaic zero-grade? This would be quite remarkable for a static paradigm. It therefore cannot be altogether excluded that the Indo-European word was PD: *u̯és-r/*us-én, where the weak stem yielded PG *uz-en, which could give *ur-.

<!-- page 50 -->

of the root *pleth2- ‘to spread out’, NIL:564ff.). I agree with Kloekhorst (HIL:763) that the most likely candidates for the original formation are *peth2- ‘to fly’, and *peth2- ‘to spread out’ and that these are one and the same root. We should then reconstruct a PD1 heteroclite *péth2- /*pth2-én-, because the root *peth2- ‘to fly, to spread out’ is semantically the most likely candidate and the *h2 is required for the coloring of the suffix vowel in Hittite. We must nevertheless allow for the possibility of confusion with the formally and semantically very similar roots *peth2- ‘to fly, to spread out’ as well as *peth1- ‘to fall’ in this word. The inherited word for ‘feather’ is attested in ON f ǫ, OE feðer, OS fethara* [f. n-st] (only as NOM.PL uetherun in Cod. Vind. 751, p.188v, r.17-22, and in the compound DAT.PL federhamon ‘feather-garment’, Hêliand 5800), OFri fethere, ODu. fethera, early MDu. vedere, vedre all meaning ‘feather, pinna’. These forms invariably go back to PG *feþr-ō. In OHG we find the cognate form fedara, federa [f. ō-st] ‘feather (of birds), quill, pinna’, cf. Cimbrian (SCHM) vedera [f.] (also vedara, NOM.PL vedarn), Cimbrian of Lusern (TYRO) vedar, NOM.PL vedarn, in Kanton Schaffhausen (WANN) fεdərə, Bündner Herrschaft (MEIN) fε dərə, Jaun, Kanton Freiburg (STUC) fædra, Appenzell (VETS) fεdərə, etc. The variation in the suffix of OHG fedara, federa is continued in Cimbrian and may therefore perhaps be real, and not mere spelling variation. The variation is parallel to the variation in the word for ‘liver’ (see §2.2.4, p.43) and perhaps reflects inherited suffix gradation S(e: ∅), from pre-OHG *feþ(e)r-. If the vowel alternation is original we would expect the suffix to have had *-a-, however, because of the final laryngeal *h2 of the root *peth2- that would have colored a following *e. The vowel timbre may on the other hand be secondary, in analogy to the (originally heteroclite) word for ‘liver’, *lib-(é)r-ō. Analogy to the word for ‘liver’ is all the more attractive, because this can explain why *feþr-ō is an ō-stem too, which is otherwise unexplained. Furthermore, a collective formation can be reconstructed as *f - an ‘plumage’, deduced from e.g. ON fiðri, OE fiðere, OHG ge-federi, MDu. ge-vedere plumage’. Finally, in the continental WG dialects we find two remarkable formations employing the complex suffix PG *-ak-. The first formation is found in OHG fedarah, fedrah ‘wing, pinion’ (the form survives in Lusern (ZING) federrich) corresponding to ODu. GEN.PL fetheracco, fetheraco ‘of wings’ (which degenerated into MDu. vlederic, Du. vlerk ‘wing, rude man’, clearly influenced by the verb MDu. vlieghen ‘to fly’). The second formation is found in OHG fettah ‘wing, pinion’ (Isidor has the ACC.PL fethdhahha), MHG vittech, vettach (the form survives in e.g. Gottschee (TSCH) att χə, Cimbrian (Schm) Vettecha ‘wing, pinion’, Swabian (SCHT) vettach ‘wing, pinion’), HG Fittich ‘wing, pinion’, MLG (LÜBB) vittek, vitk ‘wing, pinion’ (cf. Göttingen dialect (SCHA) fitch, fittek ‘wing, pinion’, Holstein dialect (SCHÜ) Fittje ‘wing, pinion’, Bremen dialect (BnW) Fitze, Fiddike ‘ridiculous headdress’ (formally a diminutive, literally meaning ‘little (chicken) wing’), Sauerland (PILK) Fittek ‘wing, pinion’, Dortmund (SCHL) Fittken ‘wing, pinion’). From these forms we can basically deduce the two proto-forms: *feþrak- and *feþþak-/*feþþek- respectively (the original geminate *-þþ- is still visible in Isidor fethdhahha). The form *feþþak- contains a geminate which has not yet been explained (the high vowel in *feþþek- occurs in later forms and is here considered to be secondary). If we start from a heteroclite we can analyze *feþrak- as deriving from a thematized strong stem, virtual *péth12-r-o-. The stems show the typical Germanic thematization of heteroclite stems and the addition of the suffix PG *-k- that is frequent in words pertaining to

<!-- page 51 -->

animals and animal names (Krahe & Meid, 1967, III:212). It is then in principle possible that the proto-form *feþþak- somehow goes back to *péth12-n-o-, containing the weak suffix *-nand following the gradation pattern of the strong stem. The initial accent, visible in the absence of Verner’s Law, would have prohibited Kluge’s Law (a preceding accent would have yielded a geminate *-tt- from earlier *-tn-' ). However, the difference of length in the dental spirants *-þn-: *-þþ- in the forms *feþrak- and *feþþak-, may be explained as a secondary ‘associative’ replacement of a consonantal contrast that arose by Kluge’s Law. Kroonen (2011:71ff., based on Kluge, 1884: 176) explains that consonant gemination yielded consonantal opposition within the paradigm, e.g. pre-PG *gnobh-ōn/*gnobh-n-ós gave rise to +knabōn/+knappas ‘young man, servant’. Due to Kluge’s Law there arose three types of opposition in paradigms: i) T: TT, ii) Þ: TT, and iii) D: TT (cf. Kroonen, 2012a:265). The geminates in the latter two contrasts were replaced by secondary geminates in Germanic. According to Kroonen (2011:71, cf. Kluge, 1884:176), an original paradigm like +knabōn/+knapp- was replaced by both 1) +knab-ōn/+knabb- (cf. OHG knabo besides knappo), and 2) +knap-ōn/+knapp- (cf. OFri. knapa besides knappa). Both paradigms 1) and 2) resulted in a simpler contrast in which the manner of articulation was generalized and only length opposition was retained. It is therefore in principle possible that the geminate *þþ in *feþþakoriginated secondarily from a paradigm in which Kluge’s Law worked. In an attempt to derive the WG formations from an inherited heteroclite stem, I propose the following scenario:30

```text
virtual PIE PG transpose PG
STRONG *péth12-r-og- +feþrak- *feþrak-
WEAK *peth12-n-óg- +fettak- → *feþþak-
```

The strong stem *péth12-r-og- directly yields the required +feþrak- whereas the virtual weak stem *peth12nóg- directly yields +fettak-, giving a PG paradigm +feþrak-/+fettak-. This is a particularly interesting solution, for it requires the presence of a nasal and hence old heteroclite inflection within the paradigm. The strong stem retained the original form *péth12-r- including its accent to which *-og- or PG *-ak- was added. The weak stem *peth12-n-' took the gradation pattern from the strong stem, however, but must have had noninitial accent for Kluge’s Law to have worked. This accentual opposition likely echoes an older gradation pattern. The transpose forms +feþrak- and +fettak-, however, show the unwanted contrast *þ: *tt within the paradigm. This undesirable situation was remedied by implementing a new contrast *þ: *þþ, whereby +fettak- was replaced by the ‘associative’ form *feþþak-. It is striking that many (High) German dialects contain a reflex of the strong stem *feþrak- besides a form of the weak stem *feþþak- in the lexicon. This shows that the 30 It is attractive to connect Gr. πτέρυξ/πτέρυγος ‘wing’ and Skt. pata - ‘flying; bird’, suggesting a PIE derivation with *-g-. There are some serious problems to tackle, however. We may assume that Greek first replaced the direct reflex of a heteroclite stem with the usual πτέρ- and then replaced the theme vowel *-o- with -υ- in analogy to bird names like κόκκ-υξ/-υγος ‘cuckoo’, ὄρτ-υξ/-υγος ‘quail’. Skt. pata - points to PIE *peth12-en-g-. This formation shows *-go- instead of *-og-, however. It seems possible that the strong stem attached *-og- and the weak *-go-, which may be interpreted as gradation: strong *peth12r-og-: *pth12en-g-o- = -S(o)-E(∅): -S(∅)-E(o). In view of the number of assumptions we have to make to explain and connect the forms (even disregarding the semantic aspect and gradation), such a formation must remain entirely conjectural.

<!-- page 52 -->

stems may have belonged to a single paradigm that broke down in a not too distant past. The forms *feþrak- and *feþþak- can in this way be satisfactorily explained as deriving from an old heteroclite paradigm. To synopsize, the proto-form *feþr-ō found in all Germanic languages must contain *feþ-r-, immediately reflecting the strong stem *péth2- (note the absence of Verner’s Law, indicating root accent as expected for the strong stem). The WG forms *feþrak-/*feþþak- can be derived from *péth12-r-o-/*peth12-n-ó-, implying that the Germanic forms continue a characterization of an old heteroclite stem *péth12-r-/*pth12-én-. The presence of reflexes of both the strong and weak stems side by side in WG strongly suggests that it can not have been that far back in time that an intact heteroclite paradigm existed (albeit characterized by the Germanic *-k- suffix). The accentual variation required to arrive at the WG forms in addition supports the PD gradation of the Hittite heteroclite.

### §2.2.8 THE WORD FOR ‘SHELTER, SHED’

A word meaning ‘shelter, shed’ is widely attested and found in e.g. ON skúr (Dan. skur), OHG scū a, sciura, OS skū, OFri. skū (WFri. skuorre), ODu. skū a, etc., all meaning ‘shelter, shed, lean-to’. The word primarily indicates a lean-to shelter or some kind of simple covering (typically against a house or farm) to protect objects or animals from the rain. The meanings of ‘hut’ and ‘shed’ naturally derive from this principal sense as more complex constructions of this type. We can mechanically deduce a pre-Germanic proto-form *skuHr-. The root of OHG sciura may show a proto-form with R(é) *sk - if it doesn’t indicate i-mutation of *skuHr-. Furthermore, we find forms with a similar meaning which have a nasal instead of the liquid *-r in OHG. The OHG forms (GRAF) scuginna and scugin gloss scuria ‘shed, stall’ (itself a medieval Latin loan from the Germanic word), and skugina glosses tugurium ‘shack, simple dwelling’, cf. MHG schiune, LG Schüne, HG Scheune ‘shed’. The OHG forms scuginna, scugin, skugina must represent phonological /skuin/, with a hiatus filling glide (cf. §3.1.3, p.62 on the spelling). The word has a parallel in ON skygni [m.] ‘shelter, hideout’, Norw. dialect skyggne [n.] ‘hut, shed, corner for shelter’, all from PN *skuggin- from earlier *sku in- (cf. AEW). Both the High German and the Nordic forms can be seamlessly derived from the proto-form *skuh1-én- (yielding PN *skuggin- by gemination, cf. Müller, 2007:293f.), with *h1 to prevent coloring of the vowel. This indicates an original root *ske h1- to which I return below. De Vaan (1999, cf. Müller, 2007:292ff.) wants to connect the words *skū - ‘shed, shelter’ and *skū - ‘rain shower’, and proposes that this was originally one and the same word. De Vaan explicitly rejects the connection of the Germanic word for ‘rain shower’ (as found in e.g. Go. skura ‘storm’ (skura windis for λαῖλαψ ἀνέμου ‘fierce gale or storm of wind’ in Mk. 4:37 and Lk 8:23), OE scū, ON skúr, ‘shower’, etc.) to the usually adduced Lat. caurus ‘northwest wind’ (< *k h1 -er-, EDLIL:100), OCS s ъ ‘north, north wind’ < *k eh1 o-, Lith. š a [f.] ‘north’, š a s [m.] ‘north wind’, š a a- ‘cold, northernly,

<!-- page 53 -->

raging, penetrating’ < *k eh1 r-, and Arm. sirt ‘hoarfrost bringing wind’ < *k eh1 -d-, and ccurt ‘cold; shower’ < *k h1ur-d-, (EDAIL:578), to a root *(s)k eh1 -. Instead, De Vaan derives the proto-form *skū - from the root *sker- ‘to cut, crack’ with the secondary ablaut of long *ū replacing the expected zero-grade *skur. This is objectionable in my opinion. First of all, the semantic connection between ‘shed, shelter’ and ‘rain shower’ is entirely unconvincing. This connection is especially objectionable since *skū - ‘rain shower’ can, despite De Vaan’s objections, easily be connected to the root *(s)k eh1 -. De Vaan (1999:40f.) thinks that a connection between a root meaning ‘north’ to ‘shower’ is not likely. However, there is no good reason for the root *(s)k eh1 - to have meant simply ‘north’. Instead, the root may very likely have meant ‘(harsh) northwestern wind’. Only Lith. š a [f.] meant simply ‘north’, whereas all other cognates clearly have connotations to harsh, cold wind, rain or storm. This recurring sense must have been original. Furthermore, De Vaan objects that the western wind is far more likely to bring bad weather than the northern wind and that therefore the root, allegedly meaning ‘north’, cannot be related. However, this objection dissolves when we reconstruct an original meaning ‘northwestern wind’, as found in Lat. caurus ‘northwestern wind’. The meaning ‘(harsh) northwestern wind’ developed in the Balto-Slavic to ‘(harsh) northern wind’ (and only secondarily to ‘north’) and in Germanic into the ‘(harsh) western wind’ that naturally brings bad weather, and then to ‘rain shower’. The original meaning of the root *(s)keh1u- must therefore have been ‘(harsh) northwestern wind’. The root can *(s)keh1u- semantically at least be easily connected to the Germanic form. Secondly, the form *skū - ‘shed, shelter’ must be formally strictly separated from the word *skū - ‘rain shower’ as a homonym. De Vaan’s assumption of secondary ablaut of long *ū replacing the expected zero-grade *skur- (a phenomenon that is very poorly understood in itself) in *skū - ‘rain shower’ is not very appealing. Furthermore, the meaning of *skū - ‘rain shower’ can semantically hardly be connected to the root *sker- ‘to cut, crack’, which requires an enormous leap. The word *skū - ‘rain shower’ must therefore be connected to *(s)keh1u- ‘(harsh) northwestern wind’. The connection of the form *skū - ‘shed, shelter’ to a root *skeuH- ‘to cover’ (thus EWN) is both semantically and formally convincing. There is no artificial explanation required for the semantics, nor do we have to assume some kind of secondary gradation when we start from the root *skeuH- ‘to cover’. As discussed above, the laryngeal may be reconstructed as *h1, and therefore I posit the more exact reconstruction *sk h1- ‘to cover’. Note that the same root is found in derivatives with *-l- in Germanic: ON skjól ‘hiding place, barn’, MLG. schūl, schule, OFri. skūl ‘hiding place’, and outside, cf. OIr. cūl ‘hidden place, corner’, Gr. σκῦλος ‘skin, hide’ (< *’covering’), etc. The following reconstructions have been proposed above for the extant forms: *skuh1r-, *sk h1 -, and *skuh1-en. These forms can be elegantly united in a single heteroclite paradigm. The (anachronistic) PIE form of this paradigm must have been this:

```text
SINGULAR COLLECTIVE
NOM.SG *sk h1- *skuh1- → *skuh1r
GEN.SG *skuh1-én-s *skuh1-n-ós
```

<!-- page 54 -->

If *sk h1r- is real (i.e. if OHD sciura does not show i-mutation of *skuh1r-) it is possible that *sk h1- formed a ō-stem: PG *sk - ō- (i.e. an abstract in *- h2?), which can account for the diphthong (we find OHG sciura, but without the factor *-i- we would have found something like **sceora). The weak stem *skuh1-én- is especially probative for a heteroclite reconstruction, for both the nasal suffix and the gradation pattern can now be explained and placed besides the strong stems *sk h1 - and *skuh1r-. That is, the heteroclite reconstruction explains at once the occurrence of the *-r-: *-n- alternation as well as the gradation patterns that go along with them. Note that the form *skūr- must have had initial accent (for otherwise the pretonic vowel would have shortened by Dybo’s Law), which can now be explained from a thematization of the double-zeroed form *skúh1r.31 This formation must have derived from the earlier strong collective stem *skuh1-, like *puh2 from*ph2 - r, cf. §3.2.1.5, p.77. I return to the development of this form in §6.1, p.123. 31 Although it cannot be excluded that the double-zeroed form *skuh1r- is the strong singular stem that took weak R(∅), an analogical change is always inferior to phonetic explanations. Moreover, the leveling must have occurred in PIE, for it requires the metathesis of laryngeal (to explain the length). Since we would expect the form *skuh1r- to exist in PIE anyway and since the regular strong stem *sk h1 - is also posited, analogy does

not seem plausible.

<!-- page 55 -->

# Chapter 3. THE HETEROCLITE NOUN FOR ‘FIRE’

## §3.1 THE GERMANIC MATERIAL FOR THE WORD FOR ‘FIRE’

In this chapter I discuss the inherited heteroclite noun for ‘fire’. It seems that Indo-European had two words for ‘fire’: *h1n gw-ni-s (cf. Lat. ignis, Lith ugnìs, etc. < *h1n w-ni-s) and *péh2, which is discussed in this chapter. According to Gamkrelidze & Ivanov (1995:238, 605), the two different forms reflects a binary classification of nouns as inactive and active. The form *h1n w-ni-s had active force and it is no surprise that the very important Rg veda deity n ḥ (< *h1n w-ni-s) continues the active word. Whereas the active noun *h1n w-ni-s did not survive in Germanic, the inactive heteroclite noun *péh2 did. The conception was in no way still of an inactive force among Germanic peoples and Grimm speaks explicitly of the conception of fire as an animate being (Grimm, 1844:567f. “ein lebendiges wesen”). In the following paragraphs I discuss the Germanic material for the heteroclite noun for ‘fire’. Then, in §3.2, p.72, I discuss the comparative Indo-European material and reconstruct the PIE paradigm. Finally, in §3.2.3, p.72 I provide a sketch of the development of the Germanic forms from the PIE heteroclite paradigm. I now turn to the material of the individual Germanic languages, starting with Gothic.

### §3.1.1 GOTHIC

Gothic possesses a neuter paradigm with a strong stem fon and a weak stem funin‘fire, πῦρ’ occurring a total of 19 times. The strong stem fon (NOM/ACC.SG) occurs 11 times and the weak stem funin- 8 times (GEN.SG funins, DAT.SG funin).32 The word has an irregular, uninflected strong stem fon and inflects in the weak cases like the neuter n-stems, cf. hairt-o [n., n-st.] ‘heart’ with the weak stem hairt-in-, augo [n., n-st.] ‘eye’ with a weak stem aug-in-, etc. The weak stem should therefore be analyzed as fun-in- attaching the suffix -in- to the root fun-. The vowel length of <u> in the root fun- cannot be ascertained from the Gothic material, however, because the Gothic writing system does not distinguish length for the grapheme <u>. In my opinion the word cannot be separated from ON funi ‘fire’ (cf. §3.1.2, p.57 below), 32 The strong stem (NOM/ACC.SG) fon occurs in Mt. 7:19, 25:41, Jo. 15:6, Lc. 3:9, 9:54, Mc. 9:22, 9:43, 9:44, 9:45, 9:46, 9:48, and the weak stem: GEN.SG funins in Mt 5:22, Mc. 9:49, R. 12:20, Th.II 1:8, and the DAT.SG funin in Lc. 3:16, 3:17, 17:29, Mc. 9:47.

<!-- page 56 -->

which has a short vowel. This would strongly suggest that the root fun- contains a short vowel. Nevertheless, the vowel may have been long if the form LG form füünsk is cognate (see immediately below). There is no transparent connection between the gradation of the strong stem fon and weak stem fun-in-. The Gothic word has long been connected to the PIE r/n-stem *péh2 *ph2uén- and it is likely that the innerparadigmatic alternation fon/fun-insomehow continues the ancient heteroclite stem alternation. The derivation of fon/fun-infrom the PIE heteroclite is investigated further in the discussion in §3.2.3 on p.83 below. Gothic has an adjective funisks* ‘fiery, flaming, πεπυρωμένος’ (a hapax occurring in Eph. 6:16 in the form of a f.ACC.PL funiskos). The Greek Vorlage has the adjectival form f.NOM.PL πεπυρωμένα ‘flaming, fiery’, a reduplicated perfect participle to the passive verb πυρόω ‘to burn with fire, to burn up’.33 The phrase of Eph. 6:16 in which the word occurs is:

```text
πάντα τὰ βέλη τοῦ πονηροῦ πεπυρωμένα
all the darts–n.NOM.PL the evil-GEN.PL flaming–n.NOM.PL
σβέσαι
to extinguish-AOR.ACT.INF
“to extinguish all the fiery darts of the wicked”
```

This phrase is translated in the Gothic Bible as follows: allos a ƕaznos s ns l ns f n skos afƕap an all the darts–f.NOM.PL the evil-GEN.PL fiery–f.NOM.PL to extinguish-AOR.ACT.INF

“to extinguish all the fiery darts of the wicked”

The Gothic text seems to gloss the Greek Vorlage word for word, where πεπυρωμένα [n.] corresponds to Go. funiskos [f.] ‘flaming/fiery’ entirely as a derivative of the word for ‘fire’ (save for the gender concord). The PERF.PRT πεπυρωμένος* occurs elsewhere in the NT, but these passages have not survived in Gothic and we cannot ascertain whether we are dealing with an ad hoc creation or calque, or with an actual Gothic colloquial formation. It seems not unlikely that fun-isks* is formed to the root of the weak stem fun- ‘fire’ and then modeled after πεπυρωμένος*, which itself is formed to the Greek word for ‘fire’ πῦρ (weak stem πῠρ-). An objection could be made, however, that the Greek word is a participle (used adjectivally), whereas the Gothic form is a straightforward adjective. Gothic funisks* is a formation with the suffix -isk- to a root fun-. The root fun- is prima facie difficult to separate from the root of the weak stem fun-in- ‘fire’ discussed above. The length of the vowel <u> in fun-, however, cannot directly be established, because Gothic orthography does not distinguish length for this letter. The vowel may have been short, as in ON funi ‘fire’ (cf. §3.1.2, p.57). On the other hand, the word may be cognate with LG f nsk ‘haughty (of girls); raging, malicious’ (cf. §3.1.5, p.68), which would indicate a long root vowel. I return to the issue in §3.2.3, p.83), where I discuss the relevant forms together. The 33 The verb πυρόω is translated by Gothic tundnan ‘to burn’, but not by a verb that is related to or derived from fon/fun-in-, cf. I Cor. 7:9 πυρoῦσθαι: Go untundnan ‘burn (with sexual desire)’, and II Co. 11:29 πυρoῦμαι: Go. tundnau ‘I burn’ (it occurs in several other passages in the Greek NT, but the corresponding Gothic bible texts have not survived). This indicates that the meaning of funisks* was not ‘burning’, but rather ‘fiery’.

<!-- page 57 -->

suffix *-isk- was used to create adjectives from collective nouns in Germanic with the meaning ‘having the character of, belonging to’ (quite similar in meaning to the English affixoid -like), cf. Go. mannisks ‘humanly, of men/mankind’, gudisks ‘godlike’ (Krahe & Meid, 1967 III:196f.). This aligns perfectly with the semantics: funisk- ‘fiery’, i.e. ‘like fire, having the character of fire’, and may corroborate that the derivational root fun- must have

meant ‘fire’.

The Gothic material contains the interesting form fon/fun-in- ‘fire’, with relic innerparadigmatic stem alternation that was otherwise eliminated in Germanic. A derivate fun-isk- from the root fun- of the weak stem is attested as well (albeit only once), which is discussed further in §3.2.3, p.83.

### §3.1.2 OLD NORSE AND THE NORDIC DIALECTS

The Nordic speech-area has a wide variety of forms. Interestingly, the word that is used most frequently is ON eldr ‘fire’ < *a l az, which in turn is derived from a weak verb *a l an- attested in e.g. OE ælan ‘to kindle, to burn’. The weak verb *a l an- may derive from *a l an- which shows dissimilatory loss of *-d- if from the root *h2e dh- ‘to ignite, inflame’ (LIV2:259, IEW 11f.), cf. Icel. eldur, Far. eldur, Norw. eld, etc. ‘fire’, and outside of Scandinavia OE æled ‘fire’, OS ēl ‘fire’. This form is obviously unrelated to the relevant heteroclite. The reflexes that interest me are the form funi and the forms fýr(r) and fúr(r), which are limited to Eddic and skaldic poetry. The form funi ‘fire (metaph.: lust)’ [m., n-stem] already occurs in the oldest Eddic and Skaldic poetry. For instance, we find the NOM.SG funi in Grímnismál 13 ǫn mk f, f n “go far from me, fire”, and in Skáldskaparmál 3811: funi kyndisk fljótt “the fire ignited quickly”. The word is continued in Icel. (ZOËG; CLEA) funi ‘fire; flame; hot-tempered man’ as well as Far. (YOUN) funi [m.] ‘dense smoke’. According to Simms (2009:318), Norn continued a reflex of funi as Shetl. fona (with variants fenna, finna, fenni, and finni) too. The word funi is, thus, a widely attested Nordic word. The form continues a NG root *fun-, which may have either been originally short or it was shortened by Dybo’s Law of pretonic shortening in early Germanic. The NG root *fun- corresponds formally and semantically to the root fun- in the Gothic weak stem fun-in-. I discuss the prehistory of the word and its connection to Go. fun-

in- in §3.2.3, p.83.

The form fúr ‘fire’ occurs in both Eddic and skaldic poetry in the earliest sources. In Skírnismál 175 and 185 the ACC.SG fúr occurs in a dialogue between the giantess Gerðr and Freyr’s messenger Skírnir:

```text
hví þú einn um komt how did you come by yourself
eikinn fúr yfir over the raging fire
ór salkynni at siá? to see our home?
```

In the final lines of the following verse (184-6) Skírnir answers Gerðr:

<!-- page 58 -->

```text
þó ek einn um komt although I did come by myself
eikinn fúr yfir over the raging fire
yður salkynni at siá? to see your home.
```

In both verses the phrase eikinn fúr ‘raging fire’ is found, which is typically interpreted as consisting of eikinn ‘violent, raging’ (to Icel. eikinn ‘unverträglich (of cattle)’, Norw. eikjen ‘quarrelsome’, Sw.dial eken ‘obnoxious’, see AEW under eikinn 1) and fúr ‘fire’. The Codex Regius manuscript (GKS 2365 4to) very clearly shows fúr with acute accent marking the length on the vowel in both verses (both lines have <eikin fv r>), cf. figure 1.a and 1.b. The other MS in which the Skírnismál is found (MS AM 748 I 4, leaf 2) clearly shows <æik in

Ϟ

fyr> in verse 17 (Skírnismál breaks off after verse 17 so verse 18 is not found in this MS), however (see figure 1.c). The superscript zigzag is a Norse convention that typically occurs in

ϟ

ua as shorthand for vera ‘to be’ and thus stands for er. Considering that elsewhere in the MS the zigzag has the sound value of either r, er, or yr 34 and that the Codex Regius has yfir following fúr (which is otherwise missing in AM 748 I 4) we may infer that the scribe intended to write fyr yfir, but that for some reason he has not done so. Although the rendering of [yfi] by a zigzag is somewhat unexpected, this practice may be explained by the possibility that the scribe forgot the word yfir and that the line ended after <fyr>. It is reasonable to assume that the scribe had already written fyr and already continued writing on the following line, but only then noticed he had forgotten the word yfir. Since the form <fyr> was close enough to the graphs used in yfir, a zigzag sufficed and the sequence fyr yfir was intelligible.

Ϟ

This would mean that the intended form actually was <fyr> representing fýr.35 But isn’t it

Ϟ

possible that the attested <fyr> stands for f[úr] y[fi]r, i.e. that the form fúr is hidden behind the contracted form?
