Since PIE *séh2 l yields *sōl by Mahlow’s Law, which would subsequently get the weak *e by analogy to the weak stem in the prehistory of Gothic, we arrive mechanically at *sō l. We might expect, however, that *sō l yields Gothic **soil, not actual sauïl. But let us first look deeper into the nature of the sequence auï. It is generally accepted that the au stands for a lower (possibly long) vowel. I disregard the controversy of the length of <au> here (i.e. whether it represents /- -/, or /- -/, or both under different circumstances), for it is quite irrelevant for our purposes. For my analysis it suffices to simply interpret au phonologically as /- -/ even though it may have been long at least in some positions. The Gothic sequence is written auï, with a diaeresis (two overdots) on the i. This does probably not, as in modern usage, indicate an independent vowel, but was used in Greek spelling to indicate a glide (Marchand, 1973). The sequence auï should therefore be interpreted phonologically as a diphthong / /. The spelling with diaeresis makes sense to indicate that the sequence of three vocalic symbols stands for a diphthong / / (and may additionally have been used to distinguish auï from the sequence awi). If sauïl stands for /s l/ we will have to explain how the sequence *-ō - in *sō(e)l became a regular diphthong / / and not something like /ō / or /ō /. The trigraph auï occurs in some interesting morphological alternations that shed light on its distribution and history, especially its alternation with oj and its relation to awi. There has been some confusion between awi and auï in the literature. For instance, Krause (1968:83) spuriously compares taujan ‘make, fashion’ (PRET. tawida) with tauï ‘deed’

<!-- page 110 -->

(GEN.SG tojis), and lumps awi and auï together. Formally and semantically the forms are very close, but if we look closely we see that the two words each show a different but consistent pattern. The diphthong in the verb taujan (PRET. tawida) shows -auj- before a vowel and -awibefore a consonant, whereas tauï shows -oj- before a vowel and otherwise -auï- before word end. Thus, one type alternates -aujV-: -awiC- (or -awi#) within the paradigm and the other type alternates -ojV-: -auïC- (or -auï#) within the paradigm. We can thus form two correspondence sets (forms of which one environment is attested are left out):60

```text
C C
-aujV-: -awi -ojV-: -auï
# #
gaujis: gawi stojan: stauïdes
haujis: hawi toja: tauï
maujos: mawi
taujan: tawide
```

The evidence for these forms is perhaps not based on many forms, but all forms amply and securely attested (especially the verbs taujan and stojan) and always perfectly consistent. Each pair of innerparadigmatic variations, then, must reflect a bifurcation in the development of a specific, single sound sequence in a particular environment. Moreover, the two different paradigmatic patterns must be kept apart and are etymologically distinct. It follows that in the case of taujan (PRET. tawida) and tauï (GEN.SG tojis) we must be dealing with two different roots (or at the very least with very distinct formations). In the supplement to LIV2, a root *deh1u- is posited to unite the words, which now seems unlikely. However, before we can decide on the matter, we require a standard or measure of certainty. Since we have established two patterns, we can interpret words of which only one variant is attested and deduce their unattested variation. A form with a reliable etymology is the NOM.PL.PRT.PF af-mauïdai ‘(they are) tired’, which must reflect the root *meh3- ‘to make work; to (be a) burden’ (LIV2:425), but of which no corresponding form af-mojV-* is found. It is remarkable that the root does not have a labial, which is in line with the interpretation of auï as / / (and was therefore traditionally called a “verbum purum”). The verb must reflect a present PG *mō- an- < *méh3- - or causative formation PG *mō- an- < *moh3- - (cf. OHG muoen ‘tire, torment’, OFri. mō a ‘bother, prosecute’, etc.). The verb stojan ‘to judge’ may show the same absence of labial. It is likely that the word stojan derives from the root *steh2‘to stand, to place’ (cf. LIV2:590 for this reconstruction), from a causative *stoh2-éie- ‘make stand’ > PG *stō- an- ‘judge’, for which no labial element is required. From these etymologies we can establish with relative certainty that the Gothic pattern -ojV-: -auïC/#reflects an earlier sequence *-ō - that developed differently before a vowel and before a consonant or word end. The other set of forms consists of words that are etymologically quite uncertain. Philippa (EWN) reconstructs a proto-form *k h2- ‘to hit’ (with transposition of the laryngeal from a root otherwise reconstructed as *keh2u-) on the basis of the Verschärfung ON hǫ a ‘hit, beat’ (cf. LIV2:345f. to 2.*keh2u- ‘strike, split’). The word hawi/haujis ‘hay’ must then go back to *ha - a- ‘that which is hewn’, showing a short vowel and labial. The origin of this pattern, then, lies in PG *-a -. 60 The forms are: gawi ‘shire’, hawi ‘hay’, mawi ‘girl’, taujan ‘make, fashion’, stojan ‘to judge’, tauï ‘deed’. Outside of these patterns falls the type naus/nawis ‘dead body’.

<!-- page 111 -->

The form gawi/gaujis ‘shire’ has also not been given a satisfactory etymology, although I believe one is readily available. The form gawi finds cognates in all Germanic branches, although mostly from place names and its precise meaning is therefore quite hard to establish (something approximating ‘shire’), cf. OHG gawi, gewi, gowi besides gawa, gawia, etc., OS - ō, -, OE - ē, OFri. ga, -gō. Phillipa (EWN) states that the etymology is unknown and tentatively suggests a contracted form of *ga-agwja- ‘belonging to water’, which is artificial and does not seem likely. However, a plausible etymology lies in the meaning found for the word in MDu. gou, (PL ghouwe) ‘path alongside water or fosse’, early modern Du. gouw ‘dike along a fosse or canal’ (Kiliaan), etc. (of which Phillipa also holds that it is the same word). The connection with a place (or path) alongside water may very well connect the word to the root * h - ‘to pour’ (LIV2:179). We know that the extended root * h -d- was used for place names (e.g. Gotland, Götaland, etc.) and for the people originating from such a place: the Goths, ON gutar < *gut- < * hu-d- with R(∅). It is, then, entirely possible that the word * a - a- derived from * h - → (anachronistic) verbal abstract * ho - o- ‘place where water is’, which would be an ideal place for a settlement (> Go. gawi ‘shire’). This would mean that gawi/gaujis derives from PG * a - a- and that we find that the pattern -aujV-: -awiC/#- continues an earlier *-a -. We can now securely establish the two patterns of innerparadigmatic variation as having developed as follows:

```text
PG Go.
*-ō - > -oj- __V
*-ō - > -auï- __C
*-a - > -auj- __V
*-a - > -auï- __C
```

This shows that the Gothic sequence -auï- derives from *-ō - before a consonant, which is important for our discussion of this word. But before returning to the word for ‘sun’, I will tie up some loose ends first. The relation between taujan ‘make, fashion’ (PRET. tawida) and tauï ‘deed’ (GEN.SG tojis) can now be elucidated. The verb taujan must come from a proto-form *ta - an- and the noun tauï from a o-stem *tō- a-. As stated above, the forms are related to the root *deh1u‘put together, arrange (in order)’ in the supplement to LIV2, but this is now impossible. The noun tauï must derive from a proto-form of the shape *deH-, however. It is possible to connect the word to the root *deh1- ‘to bind (together)’ as a deverbal io-stem (anachronistically) *doh1- o- ‘the act of binding’ > tauï ‘deed’ (cf. ON poetic tœ [n.] ‘help’). It is now also possible to connect tauï ‘deed’ to tewa ‘order, arrangement’, since the *h1 allows us to reconstruct a long ē. The formation was then (anachronistically) *deh1- h2, cf. perhaps ON tá ‘dyad’ (Snorra Edda only). Semantically, tewa ‘order, arrangement’ can be explained as the act or result of binding or putting together (i.e. an order or arrangement of things). The form taujan must have had a short vowel diphthong so that a proto-form *ta - an- can be derived from a PIE causative * o h2- - (following Philippa, EWN, to a

<!-- page 112 -->

root *de h2- ‘put together’, LIV2:123), cf. runic PRET.1SG tawido /ta ō/ on the Gallehus horn (early 5th c.), showing a short vowel as well.61

—

I now return to our word for sun in Gothic, specifically to the strong stem sauïl. From the above discussion, the spelling auï, which stands for /- -/, must go back to PG *-ō - before consonant. Thus, we can mechanically reconstruct a proto-form *sō l for Gothic sauïl. Taking into consideration that *e > i everywhere in Gothic, we may posit that *sō l > *sō l > sauïl, where *sō l took the *e analogically from the weak stem *s n- (reflected in ON syn or sýn). In this way we can unite the Gothic form with the Nordic and West Germanic forms and posit a strong heteroclite stem for Proto-Germanic: *sōl. PG *sōl is the direct reflex of the PD1 strong stem *séh2 l. Thus, the Gothic strong heteroclite stem goes back to a PD1 strong stem, which makes it likely that the weak stem *sunn- is related to the PD1 weak stem. The transpose of the weak stem was PG *s n- as I reconstructed earlier, here reproduced for convenience:

```text
SINGULAR PG TRANSPOSE
STRONG *séh2 l > +sōl
WEAK *sh2uén- > +sēn-
COLLECTIVE PG TRANSPOSE
STRONG *suh2l > +sūl
WEAK *suh2-n-' > +sun-
```

Like in the word for ‘fire’, a stem of the structure * n- was analyzed as an n-stem * -en-, i.e. as *s -en-. The root structure * - was not allowed in PG and required a remedial consonant (cf. §6.1, p.123). Like in the word for ‘fire’, the weak collective stem *suh2-n-' > +sun- (by Dybo’s Law of pretonic shortening) infected or was inserted into *s -en- to obtain the phonotactically acceptable *sun-en-. A collective stem for a single phenomenon may at first blush not sound convincing. However, it would certainly have been possible to refer to a single object with a collective (albeit more vaguely; the Greek heteroclites often reflect collectives which functioned like singulars, cf. Gr. NOM.SG ὕδωρ ‘water’ = Hitt. NOM.PL / ə /, see §2.2.2, p.38). Furthermore, the sense would still have been singular and the remodeling was purely phonological. The new stem *sun-en- was thus formed, which would have existed besides the other weak heteroclite stems *fun-en- ‘fire’ (which gave Gothic funin-) and *brun-en- ‘source, well’ (cf. the overview of §6.1, p.123). However, the form *sun-en- is not yet the attested stem *sunn- with a geminate. It is remarkable that the Gothic weak stem for fire fun-in- doesn’t have a geminate and neither its 61 Another form that is marginally attested is af-dauïdai, of which the etymology is uncertain. If it derived from *dh - ‘move rapidly; shake’, as LIV2:149f. proposes, we would instead have expected +af-dawidai. This etymology can therefore not be upheld and its prehistory remains obscure. In principle, its ancestor would have had to be a root of the shape *dheH-. It is tempting to entertain the possibility of a connection to the root *dheh1‘put, lay down; make, render’ (LIV2:136). But was it a causative *dhoh1-éie- ‘make do’ or ‘make work’ > ‘tire’?

<!-- page 113 -->

cognate in ON funi. It seems, however, that the occurrence of a nasal geminate corresponds to the existence of an n-stem. That is, the phonotactically similar weak stem forms *sunn-, *brunn- both have a corresponding an n-stem *sunn-ōn and *brunn-ēn, whereas *fun-en- did not (it did become an n-stem in ON, but this must have been a later development since there is no trace of an n-stem in Gothic). This strongly suggests that the geminate nasal had something to do with the existence of an n-stem. In this light, it seems likely that a particular n-stem case-form will have had S(∅), yielding *sun-n-, which could then have gotten attached to the root. From the weak stem *sun-en-, and similarly for *brun-en-, absorption into the n-stem class most likely proceeded on the basis of the morphological similarity of the suffix *-en-. This means that the forms were attracted to the masculine n-stems, but not the neuters in *-ōn- or feminines (which had *-ōn- throughout the paradigm). Thus, the requirements for the n-stem class to absorb our stems are: 1) the existence of a case-form with S(∅) as well as 2) a case-form with gradation S(e). It seems to me that the only choice would be the HD2 n-stems, still reflected in e.g. Go.

```text
uhsa < *uhs-ēn:
HD2 PG n-STEM
NOM.SG *- n *sun- n
ACC.SG *- n-m *sun-én-un
GEN.SG *-n-ós *sun-n-ás
LOC.SG *-én-i *sun-én
```

The double nasal in the weak stem forms *sun-ná- was generalized as if it was an original geminate. In turn, the new HD2 n-stem influenced the heteroclite weak stem. Note that we can explain the gender of Gothic sunno as analogical to feminine n-stem stairno*, but there is no model for the gender of brunna. However, the gender of brunna can be explained naturally from an HD2 paradigm as well as the origin of the geminate nasal at once.62 This is compelling evidence for this scenario. Incidentally, the HD2 types had R(∅) which corresponds closely to the shape of *sun-én- and *brun-én- (after the analysis of the forms into *Cun-én- the new ‘root’ *Cun- was doubtlessly felt to be R(∅)), which reinforces the likelihood of this scenario. In any way, we now arrive at the correct situation, which can be schematized as

```text
follows:
STAGE I STAGE II STAGE III
*séh2 l > +sōl
*sh2uén- > +sēn- → *sun-en- → *sunn-(e)n-
```

Stage I reflects the Germanic transposes of a strong stem +sōl and weak +sēn-, corresponding to +fōur/+fēn- ‘fire’. In stage II we find the (re)analysis of *sēn- as *s-ēn- with restitution of the morpheme structure to *Cun-en- on the basis of the collective. In stage III the weak 62 The gender of the WG n-stems is infamously unreliable for reconstructions, but perhaps the OHG masculine sunno ‘sun’ [m., n-st.] (besides sunna ‘sun’ [f., n-st.]) could be explained as an archaic feature, but this remains

speculative.

<!-- page 114 -->

stem of the word for ‘sun’ (as well as the word for ‘well, source’, but not the word for ‘fire’) was transferred to the HD2 n-stems, whence the geminate nasal. The development is further corroborated by the parallel development of the word for ‘well, source’, which I discuss in the

following chapter.

<!-- page 115 -->

# Chapter 5. THE HETEROCLITE NOUN FOR ‘WELL, SOURCE’

## §5.1 THE GERMANIC MATERIAL FOR THE WORD FOR ‘WELL, SOURCE’

### §5.1.1 GOTHIC

The hapax NOM.SG brunna [m, n-st.] translates Gr. πηγή ‘fountain, spring, well’ in Mk 5:29 (sa brunna bloþis izos ‘the fountain of her blood’ for ἡ πηγὴ τοῦ αἵματος αὐτῆς) in the Gothic bible. The passage in the Greek Vorlage uses the word πηγή to metaphorically describe the flow, flux, or stream of blood. Elsewhere in the bible the word πηγή has literal meaning, but these passages have regretfully not survived in Gothic. The Gothic form brunna may then translate ‘flow, flux’, i.e. the intended metaphorical meaning, but it is seems far more likely that the antecedent concept (or ‘source domain’ in modern terminology) of the metaphor itself was translated. The abundant cognates in the other Germanic dialects support the meaning ‘source, well, spring’, e.g. brunnr ‘source, well, spring, streaming water’, OHG brunno ‘source, well; streaming water’. Interestingly, Crimian Gothic has <brunna> glossing fons ‘fountain, spring’, securing the hapax Gothic form. The meaning of Lat. fons ‘fountain, spring’ and Gr. πηγή ‘fountain, spring, well’ indicates that the Gothic form meant ‘fountain, spring’. It remains uncertain whether brunna meant ‘source, well’ as well, although this seems likely. In all, Gothic brunna and Crimean Gothic brunna provide evidence for an East-Germanic masculine n-stem.

### §5.1.2 OLD NORSE AND THE NORDIC DIALECTS

ON has the well-attested form brunnr ‘source, well, spring, streaming water’ [m, o-st.], with variant bruðr (cf. mannr ~ maðr ‘man’), cf. OSw. (SÖDE) brunder, brondir, bronder, brynnen, ODan. Brynd, etc. ‘source, well, spring’. The modern languages continue reflexes of ON brunnr without phonological or semantic difficulties, cf. Mod. Icel. brunnur

<!-- page 116 -->

‘well, spring, source’, Danish brønd ‘source, well’, Swedish dialect (RIET) brunn, brónn ‘well, spring, source’, Älvd. (STEE) brunn [m.], etc. The ON word occurs in prose as well as in poetry, e.g. Hávamál 1113 DAT.SG urðarbrunni ‘well of Urðr’, corresponding to ǫl sp 198 DAT.SG urðar brunni ‘well of Urðr’, and ǫl sp 2810 DAT.SG Mímis brunni ‘well of Mímir’. It is curious, however, to find an ON masculine o-stem where all other Germanic dialects have a masculine n-stem. It is possible that the word was absorbed into the o-stems by analogy to the formally very similar unnr ‘wave’ [m, o-st.]. The associations of brunnr ‘well, spring, source’ as well as unnr ‘wave’ to (moving) water brings the words close semantically as well. From a Germanic point of view, the form does not pose any difficulties and can be connected to the common Germanic stem *brunn-. The prehistory of this stem is discussed in detail in §5.2.2, p.115121. In conclusion, then, ON has a masculine n-stem form brunnr ‘well, spring, source’, reflected in the later dialects as well.

### §5.1.3 OLD HIGH GERMAN AND THE LATER HIGH GERMAN DIALECTS

In the High German speech area we find OHG brunno, (Bavarian) prunno [m, n-st.] ‘source, well, streaming water’. The form is attested in e.g. the H.Mb. <unzi themo brunnen> ‘until the source’, 2nd Wb.Mb. <unzen den brunnon> ‘whilst the well’. In glosses the DAT.PL ad prunnom, ad prunnun [m,. n-st.] are attested, as well as Prunna with Latin -a (in Schatz, 1907:117). NHG Brunnen ‘well, source’ continues MHG brunne as expected except for an added -n (leveled from the weak case forms). The Bavarian dialects with initial p have e.g.

Imst (SCHA) prunnə, Karnten (LEXE K) prunne [m.], Laurein, Südtirol, (KOLL) pr nən [m.],

and Mòcheno (ROWL) prʊn. In the High Alemannic dialects we find e.g. Kanton Schaffhausen (WANN) brunnə, Appenzell (VETS) brɔnə, Bündner Herrschaft (MEIN) brunə, and in Jaun, Kanton Freiburg, (STUC) brunə. The Highest Alemannic dialects (Walliser German) present us with e.g. Kanton Glarus (STRE) brʊnnə, Kerenzer dialect (WINT) brunn& [m., n-st.], Visperterminen (WIPF) brunno, in the non-Walliser Heimattal (BOHN) bronne(n) [m., n-st.], but in Wallis and to the south brunna (DAT.SG brunno), and in Bosco Gurin (RUSS) Brunnä. In the Cimbrian dialects of Siben Komoin we find (SCHM) prunno, prunne, pruned [m., n-st.], a valuable living paradigm is attested in Roana (SCHW) prunno [m., n-st.] (DAT/ACC.SG prönnen, PL prunnen, all still in use today), and in Lusern we find (BACH) prun

(TYRO has prunn).

In place names we find e.g. Caldebrunna (846 AD), Breidenbrunno (817 AD), Brunnon (820 AD), Patuprunno (765 AD), Prunnon (818 AD), Chaltinprunnin (806 AD), etc. (in Küppersbusch, 1931-2). However, a form with r-metathesis, burn- or born-, is quite ubiquitous in place names as well. Especially interesting are forms that have both brunn- and burn-, cf. Sunnobrunnon next to Sunnoburnon (both 890 AD), Caldebrunna (846 AD) next to Caldenburne (893 AD), Brunike (808 AD) next to Burneche (893 AD), etc. Küppersbusch (1931-2) explains that the metathesis originates in the north and spreads southwards, after

<!-- page 117 -->

which it slowly recedes. Metathesis eventually affects most of the High German area save most Bavarian regions. The form burn-/born- can thus be explained from *brunn- by r-metathesis (with simplification of geminate nasal -nn-). In conclusion, OHG as well as its later dialects straightforwardly points to a masculine n-stem noun from PG *brunn- like all other Germanic languages.

### §5.1.4 OLD DUTCH AND THE LATER FRANCONIAN DIALECTS

ODu. has the form brunno ‘well, source, stream’ [m, n-st.], cf. DAT.PL <brunnon> (in <herren fan brunnon Israhelis> ‘from the sources [=originating] of Israel’, W.Ps. (ms. I) 067,27 from the 10th century), NOM.SG <brunno> (in <ther brunno is besigelat> ‘the well is sealed’, LW 067,11 from ± 1100), DAT.SG <brunnen> (in <mit themo besigaladen brunnen> ‘with the sealed well’, LW 067,09 from ±1100). In MDu. the form brunne continues ODu. brunno, but metathesized forms such as borne, borre take over in this period. However, in place names the metathesized form already occurs in the ODu. period, cf. Burne (1125 AD), Bornehensis (1110 AD). The Du. form bron (cf. Limburgian brón) is taken to be influenced by German Brunnen (EWN). ODu. thus points to brunno ‘well, source, stream’ [m, n-st.] with a metathesized variant burne* (of which the gender is hard to ascertain) typically occurring in place names.

### §5.1.5 OLD SAXON AND THE LOW GERMAN DIALECTS

OS has brunno* ‘source, well; spring water’ [m, n-st.], fragmentarily attested in in the Hêliand, e.g. line 1967 has the GEN.SG brunnan (in M), brunnon (in C), and line 3919 has the

NOM.PL brunnon (in both M and C). C has the odd ACC.SG brunnion in line 5473, with the

unexpected ending -ion containing -i- (the concord proves the masculine ACC.SG <hluttran brunnion> ‘clear spring water’). Schlüter (1892:243) interprets the form as one of many forms with ‘derivational i’ (“das ableitende i”) and assumes influence of ja-stems, which may explain this aberrant form. MLG (LÜBB) has the metathesized form borne [m.] ‘source, well, fountain; fresh (spring) water’, cf. the older LG dialect of Pommerania and Rügen (1800, DÄHN) born [f.] ‘well, source’, the Dortmund dialect (SCHL) bo n ‘well, source, spring’, Kurköln dialect, Sauerland (PILK) buarn ‘well, source, spring’, etc. The forms in the dialects all continue MLG borne regularly. Some LG dialects retain a meaning that is found in the MLG weak verb (Lübb) bornen ‘lead (cattle) to the well, fresh water, or drinking trough’. The ‘well, source’ or ‘fresh (spring) water’ was the place where cattle drank their water and from this the MLG weak verb bornen developed. The meaning of the verb was later transferred to

<!-- page 118 -->

the noun born, which got the sense of ‘the drinking trough for cattle’, cf. the Göttingen dialect which, like MLG, still has (SCHA) Born ‘source, well, fountain; spring water’ besides the verb börnen ‘lead to water; to water (animals)’, whereas the older Holstein dialect has (1800, SCHÜ) Born ‘drinking trough’ and the verb börnen ‘leading cattle to the drinking trough’, the older Bremen dialect has (1767, TILI) Born-blok ‘the trough from which cattle drinks’ and the verb bornen ‘to water cattle’ (but the original sense still in e.g. Born-grund ‘welling, boggy soil’, Quik-born ‘well, source, spring; well area’), and the older Hamburg dialect (1755, RICH) has the verb börnen ‘leading cattle to the drinking trough and (independently) giving milk to calves’. The Altmark dialect has the form (DANN) spring-brunn ‘well, source’ without metathesis, which may point to influence from High German or may be a form without

r-metathesis.

### §5.1.6 OLD ENGLISH AND THE BRITISH DIALECTS

OE has the metathesized variants burne [f, n-st.], burna [m, n-st.] ‘a stream, river; bubbling water’, also occurring in place names e.g. B nstōw. The form brunna [m, n-st.] can be deduced from place names, e.g. Denisaesbrunna. Modern Scots and North English dialects continue the word burn ‘a small stream, brook’, southern English and American English dialects have bourne ‘a brook, rivulet’, both deriving from ME burne, bourne ‘a natural small

river’.

### §5.1.7 OLD FRISIAN AND THE FRISIAN DIALECTS

OFri. has burna ‘source, well’ [m, n-st.], which is the metathesized variant occurring predominantly in OE as well in most of the WG dialects. Mod.Fri. boarne continues OFri. burna directly. EFri. has (OUTZ) Brunn ‘source, well’ does not show metathesis and may be a loan from Danish or LG. The word is in modern languages typically replaced by other words meaning (or associated with) ‘source, spring, fountain, well’. For instance, Johannes Cadovius Müller (1691, KÖNI here) has soohde for ‘source, well’ (WFri. (WFT) saed) as well as putte for ‘draw well’ (cf. WFri. (WFT) pet, put).

## §5.2 THE PIE ORIGINS OF THE WORD FOR ‘WELL, SOURCE’

<!-- page 119 -->

### §5.2.1 THE ETYMOLOGICAL CONNECTION OF THE PIE HETEROCLITE STEM ‘WELL, SOURCE’

#### §5.2.1.1 ANCIENT GREEK AND ARMENIAN

Greek continues a living heteroclite φρέᾱρ/φρέᾱτος [n, r/n-st.] ‘well, source, spring; tank, cistern’, NOM.PL *φρέᾱτα (spelled φρείατα), contracted to φρητός (Egypt), etc. These forms go back to older *φρέϝᾱρ < *φρηϝaρ (i.e. PGr. *ph ē a ) by quantitative metathesis. The proto-form *ph ē a can be derived from *bhréh1 continuing the strong PD1 stem (cf. Beekes, 2010:1590). The Sanskrit material indicates a PD1 stem (cf. §5.2.1.2 below), but since Greek has given up the root-gradation, the PD1 gradation type cannot be ascertained on the basis of the Greek evidence alone. The Greek forms find an exact cognate in Arm. ałb w, ałbewr [r-st.] ‘source, well’, GEN/DAT.SG aɫber, ABL.SG y-aɫber-ē (cf. EDAIL:32f.). The word is ubiquitous in the Armenian dialects, cf. Moks axpör, but also with aspiration häxpür, Nor Bayazet haxpur, Ozim ha p‘, etc. EDAIL:32f. explains the initial aspiration from the prepositional phrases such as ABL.SG y-aɫber-ē. The classical form Arm. ałb w, ałbewr ‘source, well’ derives from *bhréh1, just like the Greek forms. Greek provides evidence of a living heteroclite to an ossified strong stem, which points to a virtual stem *bhréh1 n-. This reconstruction is supported by the non-heteroclite ossified strong stem reflexes of *bhréh1 in Armenian.

#### §5.2.1.2 SANSKRIT

The Indic stem bhurvan- is found in classical Skt. bhurvan- ‘restless movement of water’ and in Vedic bh - ‘turbulent, wild’ (RV). The accent in bh - indicates that the form is most likely to be a noun.63 The form is for instance found twice in RV i.134.5 where it occurs in the following lines:

máde u- ́ i a anta bhurvá i

exaltation-LOC.PL + great-N/A.PL hasten(themselves)-PRES.INJ.MED.3PL LOC.SG ‘great-in-exaltation, (...) hasten (themselves) in the [bhurváni],’ 63 Burrows (1955:142) explains that adjectives in -van- have accent on the root and an adjective would be: **bhúrvan-. This indicates that we are dealing with a noun.

<!-- page 120 -->

ap ́m i anta bhurvá i

water-GEN.PL hasten(themselves)-PRES.INJ.MED.3PL [see text]-LOC.SG ‘hasten (themselves) in the [bhurváni] of the waters’ Although the injunctive forms i a anta and i anta occur nowhere else, the passage is quite comprehensible.64 The form bhurvá i may in principle be taken as a LOC.SG of an infinitive or verbal noun. It is however not necessary to interpret the form as an ad hoc infinitive as the form is old. Before explaining all the formal details, I posit that the form bhurváni is the expected LOC.SG of the weak PD stem: *bh h1 n, of the heteroclite noun for ‘well, spring’ instead. The form bhurvá i corresponds entirely to the transpose of the PIE LOC.SG *bh h1 n and provides a remarkable direct reflex as I show below. This interpretation of the form is much more attractive than proposing an infinitive to an otherwise unattested verb. In RV i.56.12, we find a NOM.SG bhurvá iḥ, showing that Vedic Sanskrit had leveled the weak stem in favor of the strong stem throughout the paradigm. This i-stem form bhurvá iḥ is, in my view, a late formation based on the locative bhurvá i. This can at once explain the ‘delocatival’ i-stem as well as the use of the weak stem in the NOM.SG. In any way, Vedic shows that a noun bhurván- ‘turbulent, wild water’ had once existed, corresponding to classical Skt. bhurvan- ‘restless movement of water’. Let us now return to the formal

intricacies.

It is not readily apparent, though, that *bhruh1én- yields Sanskrit bhurván-. Lubotsky (1997) argues that the Vedic reflexes of PIE *CRHUV sequences can be divided into two groups: one with long ū, ī and the other with short ur, ir. Lubotsky establishes that the variation can be elegantly explained by an accent rule:

```text
ˊ ˊ
ū, ī < * (i.e. with accented liquid )
ˊ ˊ
ur, ir < * (i.e. with accented vowel V)
ˊ
```

For example, the adjective t - ‘victorious’ with short -ur- < * contrasts with

ˊ

tū - ‘superior’ with long -ū - < *, both from the root t - ‘to cross, conquer’ < *t h2-.

ˊ

The form bhurván- (Vedi bh -) must reflect * and can by the law established by Lubotsky be seamlessly derived from *bhruh1én-, with short -ur- due to the original suffixal accent. The proto-form *bhruh1én- must be the weak complement to the strong stem as found in Greek and Armenian: *bhréh1 (cf. §5.2.1.1, p.119). That is, bhurván- derives from a proto-form *bhruh1én- showing the weak PD gradation pattern R(∅)-S(é)-. In his discussion of the material, Lubotsky (1997:3) mentions bhurváni- (< the root *bh -) as a form of which only the form with a short vowel is attested. However, a thematic reflex of the strong stem *bhréh1 probably survives in Vedic as well, as already intimated by Burrow (1955:150). RV iv.21.7 contains the epithet GEN.SG bh a sya ‘of (the) 64 It seems natural to relate the injunctives i a anta and i anta to √i - ‘to stream out, pour out’ in the context of water and soma-drops (the verb probably reflects * s- ‘sieden, schäumen’, cf. LIV2:312f., or perhaps *h1 sh2‘kräftigen; antreiben’, cf. LIV2:234,). The form i anta only occurs here and may not be original (cf. Müller, 1869:cxliv), but the forms are sufficiently similar to assume that we are dealing with two instance of the same verb. The suffix -an- in i -a -(anta) remains obscure. However, the verbal form i a - can perhaps somehow be related to the noun i a - ‘streaming out, pouring out’ (DAT.SG i a i in RV ii, 2, 9), although this doesn’t shed much light on the matter. The verbs i a anta and i anta—assuming that they have the same meaning—must in any case mean ‘pour (themselves) out’.

<!-- page 121 -->

impetuous (one)’ (bh a sya aḥ ‘the bull of the impetuous one’). Semantically, the meaning ‘impetuous’ is sufficiently close to bhurván- ‘turbulent, wild water’, which then does not preclude a connection. Mayrhofer (1993:253, under the root bharv- ‘chew, digest, eat’) doubts the affinity of the words, but also rejects Grassmann’s reading of the bhurván- as ‘consuming [verzehrend]’, and rightfully so. Regardless of Mayrhofer’s reservations, it is very likely that the forms bh a á- and bhurván- are related. The form bh a á- could be a thematized heteroclite noun of the type described by Burrow (1955:135ff. and 147ff.), who finds that nouns with -vará- occur side by side with adjective in -van- and must be related to old heteroclites, cf. pī a - ‘fat’: pī an- ‘fat’, adhvará- ‘sarifice’: adhván- ‘way’, etc. Following the same pattern, the noun bh a á- occurs alongside the classical Skt. adjective bhurvan-. This pattern not only suggests that we are dealing with a heteroclite, but tells us something about the Vedic noun bhurván- as well. Because the form bhurván-, deriving from a weak PD1 stem *bhruh1én-, is actually a noun it falls outside of the Indic pattern of ‘thematic strong stem noun: weak stem adjective’. This noun itself has no indication of being derived from the adjective and must be original, i.e. it must then be an archaism. Formally, the connection between bh a á- and bhurván- is impeccable. The transpose of the PD1 strong stem *bhréh1 is Vedic +bh a. The form bh a á-, then, is nothing other than a thematization of the strong PD1 stem transpose +bh a. The form bh a á- thus corresponds entirely to Greek φρέᾱρ and Armenian ałbewr. Alternatively, one could posit that the suffix -var- took analogical S(é) from the weak stem, but this is not at all necessary. The form bh a - derives from *bhréh1, providing direct evidence for the strong PD1 form. Furthermore, there is compelling evidence for the weak PD1 form in the form bhurván- < *bhruh1én-. The systematic relation of nouns in -vará- corresponding to adjectives in -ván- (whatever their precise origin) provides more evidence that these forms once formed a heteroclite paradigm. In conclusion, we have found compelling evidence in Armenian, Greek, and especially Indic for a PD1 heteroclite noun *bhréh1 /*bhruh1én-.

### §5.2.2 THE DERIVATION OF THE PG FORMS FROM THE PIE HETEROCLITE STEM ‘WELL, SOURCE’

The Germanic languages invariably point to an n-stem proto-form *brunn-ēn or *brunn-ōn [m, n-st.] as discussed in the paragraphs of §5.1, p.115. Again I start by reconstructing the Germanic transpose of the PD1 paradigm, which yields the following

```text
result:65
SINGULAR TRANSPOSE
STRONG *bhréh1 > +b ur
WEAK *bhruh1én- > +bru n-
```

65 For the Sievers’ Law variation indicated by the superscripted ‘u’, cf. the word for ‘nine’ *(h1)n n > PG *n un, yielding Go. niun < *n n but OS nigun from *n n.

<!-- page 122 -->

The strong form would have developed as follows: *bhréh1 > +bh ē > +b ur by shortening of the long diphthong. The reflex of the weak stem may actually have been +bu n- instead of +bru n- if the liquid vocalized. However, the laryngeal must first have been lost by the law *# - > *# - (cf. Ringe, 2006:82), yielding the (anachronistic) form *bh n-. We may alternatively assume that at this point in the development of Germanic the vocalization of liquids and nasals had not yet occurred, which is not at all

unlikely.

As we’ve seen with the words for ‘fire’ and ‘sun’, stems of the form *Cunn- were formed from PD1 paradigms that analyzed *sēn- as *s-ēn-. A proto-form *br -en- is quite likely and must have existed at one time. Like in the words for ‘fire’ and ‘sun’, the stem *bruen- (with the phonotactic pattern * -en-) would have been remodeled to satisfy the required morpheme structure constraints by attaching a consonant to the root. The source for remodeling would have been the weak collective stem *bhruh1-n-' > *brun-, as with the words for ‘fire’ and ‘sun’. The development was then: *bru-en- → *brun-en-. Although the form *brun-en- is a step in the right direction, we still have to account for the geminate nasal. There is a conspicuous correlation between the existence of an n-stem form and the secondary attachment of a nasal in the reflexes of heteroclites in Gothic and the Nordic languages. That is, ‘fire’ does not get a geminate nasal in the weak stem (Go. fun-in-, ON fun-i) and the word has no n-stem counter. Conversely, the word for ‘sun’ (heteroclite in Go. sauïl/sunn-in-) has an n-stem counter (Go. sunno, ON sunna, and WG *sunn-, cf. OHG sunna, etc.) and the word for ‘source, well’ is only reflected as an n-stem (Go. brunna, cf. WG *brunn- in OHG brunno, etc., but the exceptional ON o-st. brunnr must derive from an earlier n-stem). This suggests that the geminate nasal originated in the n-stems. Thus, the geminate originates from a case-form such as GEN.SG *-n-as(a) attached to the refurbished root *Cun-. This yielded *brun-n-a, from where the nasal was attached to the root and became the new *brunn-ēn. This can elegantly explain the variation between the weak stems of the two Gothic heteroclite reflexes: fun-in- ‘fire’ (with a singulate nasal in the stem fun-) in contrast to sunn-in- ‘sun’ (with a geminate nasal in the stem sunn-). The geminate in the weak stem of the heteroclite Go. sauïl/sunn-in- then, took the geminate by analogy to the n-stem reflected as sunno [f.]. The development which ran partially parallel in our three heteroclites

```text
ran thus:
STAGE I STAGE II STAGE III
*bhruh1én- → +b n- → *brun-en- → *brunn-(e)n-
*sh2uén- > +sēn- → *sun-en- → *sunn-(e)n-
*ph2uén- > +fēn- → *fun-en-
```

Note that stage I is simply the Germanic transpose, stage II represents the analysis *C-ēn- with restitution of the morpheme structure to * n-en-, and stage II indicates the weak stems that went over into the n-stems and therefore does not apply to *fun-en-.

<!-- page 123 -->

# Chapter 6. THE DEVELOPMENT OF HETEROCLITES IN GERMANIC

## §6.1 THE PARALLEL DEVELOPMENT OF SOME GERMANIC HETEROCLITES

In this section I present an overview of the development of the heteroclite nouns for ‘fire’, ‘sun’, ‘well, source’, and ‘shelter, shed’. The scenario of the development of the weak stems can be schematically represented as follows:

```text
STAGE I STAGE II STAGE III
*bhruh1én- → *brūn- → *brun-en- → *brunn-(e)n-
*sh2uén- > +sēn- → *sun-en- → *sunn-(e)n-
*ph2uén- > +fēn- → *fun-en-
*skuh1-én- > +skuen-
```

Stage I is simply the Germanic transpose, although *bhruh1én- → *brūn- required loss of the laryngeal. Stage II reflects the reinterpretation of the stem *sēn- as *s-ēn-. The nominal root structure *CV- was not allowed in Germanic and the form therefore required remedial restructuring.66 Now, the collective weak stems of the structure *Cuh2n-' yielded *Cun- with pretonic shortening by Dybo’s Law and did exhibit a required root structure of the Germanic root morpheme structure constraints. The weak collective stems of the type *Cun- must have been the source of the remodeling. The root structure of * -en- was thus remedially restructured to *Cun-en-, either by copying the vowel or inserting the weak collective stem

into the singular.

In stage III we find that the weak stems *brunn-(e)n- ‘source, well’ and *sunn-(e)n‘sun’ developed an independent n-stem paradigm. The forms must have become a masculine HD2 n-stems with a NOM.SG in *-ēn (Go. -a) and not a feminine or neuter with a NOM.SG *-ōn 66 It appears that nouns in Germanic had a minimal root structure requirement *CnVC- (where n = zero or more). From a survey of the nouns in Bammesberger (1990) it appears that even forms where one would expect a root structure *CV- (at least seemingly) inexplicable ‘enlargements’ occur to the root, e.g. *lid-an to the root *l - + -t-/-dh- (cf. ON lið ‘army’, OE lid ‘ship’), *s k-an to the root *s - + -g- (cf. ON svik ‘treason, poison’), *brutan to the root *bh - + -d- (cf. ON brot ‘fragment’, OE gebrot ‘fragment’), *drug-an to the root * - + -gh-/ -k- (cf. OS gidrog, OHG gitrog ‘appearance’), *nut-an to the root *n - + -d- (cf. ON not ‘use’), *rub-an to the root * - + -p-/-bh- (cf. ON rof ‘downfall’), etc. Although at least some of these forms may be possibly explained, a more detailed study will have to determine on the matter. Nevertheless, the conspicuous preference for minimal *CnVC- in Germanic roots is striking, whatever the explanation.

<!-- page 124 -->

(Go. -o) for the following reasons. First of all, the form and gender of brunna is most likely to be original, because there is no plausible model from which it would have taken the gender analogically. Secondly, the gender of Gothic sunno may not have been original, but be formed in analogy to the feminine n-stem stairno*. I believe that the creation of a novel n-stem with HD2 gradation is the most likely scenario for both the weak stems *brunn-(e)n- ‘source, well’ and *sunn-(e)n- ‘sun’, because only the HD2 middle stem had a suffix *-en- corresponding to the heteroclite weak suffix *-en-. That is, both the HD2 middle stem and the PD weak stem had the shape of the suffix alternant *-en- in common, which would have facilitated the creation of an HD2 n-stem paradigm. The masculine gender and form of brunna can be explained naturally from an HD2 paradigm *brun- n (cf. Kroonen, 2011, chapter 2 on the development of n-stems). Moreover, the geminate nasal can also arise quite easily from the weak HD2 stem forms, cf. the GEN.SG *sun-n-ás and *brun-n-ás. The nasal was then felt to be part of the root and generalized throughout the paradigm. The word for ‘sun’ must, as indicated above, later have taken the feminine n-stem endings in analogy to stairno*. The word for ‘sun’, then, must have developed as follows (after the creation of an HD2 paradigm):

```text
NOM.SG *sun- n → *sunn- n
ACC.SG *sun-én-un → *sunn-én-un
GEN.SG *sun-n-ás = *sunn-ás
LOC.SG *sun-in-i → *sunn-i
```

In ON, the form funi shows that the stem for ‘fire’ eventually also became an n-stem, but it had not yet in Gothic.67 This form must therefore have originated later, probably in Proto-Nordic or later times. This is also proved by the absence of a geminate nasal in ON funi, whereas ON sunna and brunnr show a geminate from the early HD2 paradigm. The issue at hand, then, is why the form *fun-en- did not develop an n-stem counterpart. A remarkable symmetry can explain the absence of an early n-stem from the weak heteroclite stem for ‘fire’. First of all, *fuir- (e.g. disyllabic OHG <uugir> securing an actual diphthong, not a monophthongized, mutated vowel), *skuin- (e.g. disyllabic OHG <scuginna>, <skugina>, <scugin> also securing an actual diphthong, not a monophthongized, mutated vowel), and likewise possibly *suin- (ON syn or sýn) must have had *i and not *e as discussed in their respective chapters. The most natural solution is to assume leveling of the suffix *-in- from the DAT.SG *-in-i by the raising of *-én-i (cf. Boutkan, 1995:72ff.). Since the *i in place of expected *e occurs in all languages this must have occurred early on in the common proto-language. We can now effectively explain the high vowel of NwG *fuir-, *suin- and *skuin-, with *i instead of *e, by the leveling of *-in- from the DAT.SG *fuin-i, *suin-i and *skuin-i respectively. The symmetry that we find is this: the forms which retained *-en- went on to restructure the stem * -en- to *Cun-en- (stage II), whereas the forms that leveled the suffix *-in- did not. The explanation must be that the reinterpretation of * n- as an n-stem *Cu-en- explicitly required the suffix *-en-, not *-in-. This can directly explain the transfer to the HD2 n-stems, which had *-en- in the ACC.SG. We now arrive at a satisfactory 67 Even though Gothic fon/funin- has leveled the nasal to the strong stem, it is still not properly a Gothic n-stem with a NOM/ACC.SG ending -o or –a.

<!-- page 125 -->

derivation of the heteroclite reflexes of Germanic. Nevertheless, there is one standing problem with the account that I need to explain: the loss of laryngeal in the sequence *#CHu̯é-.

## §6.2 ON THE DEVELOPMENT OF THE LARYNGEAL IN THE SEQUENCE *#CHu̯é-

I believe that in the discussion of the heteroclite weak stem there is scanty, but compelling evidence for the loss of laryngeal at the beginning of a word. The form of *fuir-* ‘fire’ can hardly be convincingly explained otherwise, unless one wants to resort to *ad hoc* changes and unmotivated restructuring. The derivation of the forms *ph2u̯én > +fu̯én-* ‘fire’ and *sh2u̯én- > +su̯én-*, in combination with the symmetric development of the heteroclite for ‘source, well’, provides evidence for the loss of laryngeal in the environment *#CHUE*. I posit the following law with the more exact formulation:

4. LAW OF LARYNGEAL LOSS. – A laryngeal H is lost after a word-initial consonant and before a contiguous bilabial semi-vowel plus accented full-grade front vowel *u̯é*, so that:

```text
*H > ∅ | #C__u̯é-
```

This means that the laryngeal was not lost before #C__u̯ó- and this is exactly what we find. For instance, Go. NOM.PL *fawai*, OE *fēa* ‘few’ must go back to *ph2u̯-ó-*, in which the laryngeal survived and vocalized. However, there is a group of words that provides important counter-evidence to the law of laryngeal loss as posited above. The forms are conspicuously limited to class III weak verbs. For instance, OHG *zawēn* ‘proceed, succeed’ < PG *ta(w)-ē(a)n-* < PIE *dh1u̯-eh1-*, ON *má* ‘scrape off, wipe off’ < *mē1ēn-* < PIE *mh2u̯-eh1-*, and OHG *bawēn* ‘inhabit, cultivate’ < PG *bē1ēn-* < PIE *bhuh2-eh1-* show exceptional loss. These forms require separate treatment and are not enough to overturn the narrower formulation proposed here.

<!-- page 126 -->

*s -, where he compares Gr. ἀείρω ‘lift’ (< *ἀϝείρω) to HG Schwer, but this is impossible because the Germanic word had a long vowel, cf. OHG sw < *s ē1r-. The other example is *h2 l-: *s l-, where the (isolated) ON plural svilar ‘brothers-in-law, whose wives are sisters of each other’ is compared to Gr. ἀέλιοι ‘brothers-in-law, whose wives are sisters of each other’. But, the Greek form probably goes back to *sm -s l-io- and then does not prove loss of laryngeal either. Nikolaev’s proposal, then, does not hold. Furthermore, he attempts to connect the Germanic word for ‘sword’, *s a- (cf. ON sverð, OHG swert, OS swerd, OFri. swerd, OE sweord, etc., all meaning ‘sword’) to the form s ()al- ‘dagger’, but this form itself is quite uncertain (cf. HIL:885f.). It is also not very appealing to sever the word from PG *s - ‘pain, ulcer’ (cf. OHG swero, ODu. suero, MLG swē, etc, ‘pain, ulcerʼ), and Av. xvara- ‘wound’, OIr. serb ‘bitter’, W. chwarren ‘ulcer’, etc., as Nikolaev does. Nevertheless, the semantic connection between ‘sour, bitter, stinging’ and ‘(stinging) wound’ is attractive. Perhaps the forms going back to PIE *s - (with a range of senses: ‘wound, pain, bitter(ness), ulcer’, perhap originally ‘wound’) are related to the word for ‘sour’, *suHr- (cf. ON súrr, OHG sū, OE sū, etc., ‘sour’, related to Lith. sū as ‘salt’ (adj.), Let. s s ‘salt, bitter’). In order to connect *s - to *suHr-, we must reconstruct the stems *s er- (> *s - by the law discussed above) and *sHur- (> *suHr- by laryngeal metathesis) respectively. The stems can together go back to an old HD1 r-stem (disregarding the uncertain

```text
morpheme boundary):
NOM.SG *s
ACC.SG *s ér-m
GEN.SG *suHr-ós
```

The Germanic form *sū a- must have had root accentuation (betrayed by the absence of Dybo’s Law of pretonic shortening), which has not been explained. In my opinion, this may echo a strong stem with root accent or R(é), i.e. *séH, which was leveled to the weak stem, but this cannot be proved. If there was an r-stem in PIE it would provide another example of the loss of laryngeal in *s ér- > *s ér- and in a cross-Indo-European perspective as well. At the base of the paradigm would then be a root *se -. Nevertheless, this form cannot count as evidence, for it is too uncertain. For one, there is no trace of a strong stem *séH and furthermore it remains unexplained why the middle stem *s ér- would have been thematized (although this is perhaps not too serious an objection). Recently Byrd (2011) has argued for a revision of Pinault’s Law of laryngeal loss. Pinault (1982) observed that laryngeals were sometimes lost before *- -. For instance, while a laryngeal was present in Skt. k a - ‘raw meat’ < *k h2-s-, it was lost in kravya- < *k h2o-. He proposed the following law to account for this loss:

*H > ∅ | C -

The law has not found general acceptance in the scholarly community, but Byrd shows that despite the criticism there are nevertheless some unexplained instances of laryngeal loss before *- -. Byrd reformulates the law as follows (excluding *h1 in this formulation because of the statistical rarity of good examples, which seems questionable to me):

<!-- page 127 -->

PINAULT-BYRD’S LAW. – A laryngeal *h23 is lost in absolute onset of a syllable, before a contiguous tautosyllabic *i̯, so that

```text
*H > ∅ | σ[__i̯
```

However, Byrd notes that this does not solve all problems. For instance, there are words such as Skt. dyati < *dh1 -eti which lose *h1 just like *h2 is lost in Ved. (YV) ava-dyáti < *dh2 -eti. Nevertheless, the environment specified is remarkably similar to the one I posited and it is worth the effort to see if the law I propose is an extension of Pinault-Byrd’s Law. Since in Germanic all reflexes of laryngeals in non-initial syllables were lost, we can only find traces in initial syllable. Sadly, all examples of Pinault’s Law that are found in more than one language are instances of loss in internal syllable. However, one example of a form with loss of laryngeal in initial syllable, occurring in more than 1 language, can be found in Rasmussen (1989:61; cf. Ringe, 1996:20), who reconstructs a PD1 paradigm for the word for ‘shadow’, which shows the loss of laryngeal in the environment specified by Byrd: *sk h2 -h2 > Skt. ch y (with leveled *R(e)-S(é)-): *sk h2 -éh2- > Gr. σκία, TB skiyo ‘shadow’. Alas, this word has no cognate formation in Germanic.68 Nevertheless, the law of laryngeal loss in #CH -, as I have specified above, may very well be an extension of Pinault-Byrd’s Law in Germanic and possibly in Indo-Iranian. We have seen the loss of laryngeal in several forms, including *ph2 én > +fēn- ‘fire’ and *sh2 én- > +sēn- ‘sun’, but there two more interesting (sets of) words: the word(s) for ‘palate, gums’ and the word(s) for ‘pole’, which I discuss in more detail in the following paragraphs.

## §6.3 THE WORD FOR ‘PALATE, GUMS’

The word for ‘palate, gums’ in Germanic is attested in several different stems. A very frequent form that must go back to PG * ōm- is found in OHG guomo, ON gómr, OSw. ōm, ODan. ōmæ, OE ōma, OHG guomo, etc. ‘palate, side of the mouth, gums’. Furthermore, in OHG we find goumo < * a m- (reflected in HG Gaumen) ‘palate’. In the Low German area we find forms going back to * ūm-, cf. MLG (LÜBB) gûme ‘palate, gums’, Göttingen dialect (SCHA) gûme ‘palate, gums’, MHG (LEXE) gûme, Swiss (SI) ūm ‘palate, gums’, Swabian (SCHT) gume [f.] ‘palate, gums’, etc. A form with possibly a geminate and short vowel *gumm- is found in e.g. MHG DAT.SG gummen ‘palate’ (in Diu Crône, Cod. Pal. Germ. 374), MDu. gumme (Kiliaan), etc. Finally, another old OHG form that is found in glosses is guimo ‘palate’ (STSI; Graff falsely gives giumo, see Singer, 1886:297f.), also (STSI) giumo (2×). The sequence ui could be interpreted to represent a mutated vowel, however, 68 Unless we are really dealing with remnants from an old HD1 *-ih2-stem (the kī-type): *sk h2- h2-s/ *sk h2- h2-m /*sk h2- h2-ós (cf. Kuiper, 1942:12ff. and Beekes, 20112:201 on this type of formation). The root may then be found in *ska a- ‘shadow’ if from *sk h 2-tú- (cf. ODu. skado, OS skado, OHG skato, etc. ‘shadow’), a different derivation. The possible connection to OS skimo, OE scima, etc., ‘shadow’, is difficult. Nevertheless, the cognate formation is not attested in Germanic.

<!-- page 128 -->

since guimo is attested in the first half of the 11th century (cf. the reasoning in §3.1.3, p.62, where it is determined that only after around 1000 the spelling <ui> is coming in use to indicate a mutated and monophthongized vowel). The form would then have leveled from the dative, although I find it hard to believe that so shortly after the occurrence of mutation a mutated stem form would have been generalized. Note that the etymological connection to the root * heh2- precludes a PG * m- in giumo because of the laryngeal that would have lengthened and colored the *e (cf. the derivation below). This means that guimo may either go back to an older form with a mutated vowel or to a form with an inherited rising diphthong, which would means that that giumo must be the metathesized counterpart just as with the diphthong in fuir besides fiur ‘fire’ (cf. §3.1.3, p.62). There are, then, a total of five different stem forms for the word for ‘palate, gums’ in Germanic: * ōm-, * a m-, * ūm-, *gumm-, and

*guim-.

The word has long been connected with e.g. Lith. gomur s [m.], om om [f.] ‘throat, gullet, Adam’s apple’, Latv. gãmurs ‘larynx, windpipe’, etc. to a root * heh2- ‘gape, be wide open’, which also occurs in Gr. χά(ϝ)ος ‘primordial Chaos’ < * hh2- -, Hes. Th. 116 (cf. Lubotsky, 2011, on the root). Bjorvand & Lindeman (2000:311f.) propose to reconstruct a PIE m-stem for the Germanic forms. They derive PG * ōma- < *ghh2 -ōm- like Go. fon < *ph2 -ōm-. As discussed in §3.2.3, p.83, Go. fon must simply derive from the singular stem *péh2 (with leveling of analogical weak suffix *-n-) by Mahlow’s Law, which does not support the derivation * ōma- < *ghh2 -ōm-. However, the proposal of an m-stem to the root * heh2 - (the root * heh2- with *-u- extension) is very attractive, for it can unite the Germanic forms with the Latin form famēs. Brent Vine, in a three-day seminar on Latin etymology and phonology at the University of Copenhagen, October 2012, proposed to derive the troublesome Latin word famēs ‘hunger’ from an m-stem * hh2 -ém-. Semantically, the connection between * heh2‘gape, be wide open’ and ‘hunger’ easily can be made, for which he provides the parallel: cf. χάος ‘primordial Chaos’ → χαόω ‘destroy’ from earlier *‘devour, swallow up’. Thus, ‘devour, swallow up’ can easily be connected to ‘gape, be wide open’, and this is naturally connected to hunger and being hungry. Formally, Vine wants to derive famēs ‘hunger’ from a

LOC.SG * hh2 -ém- of a holokinetic paradigm. The vowel is here required to metathesize so

that * huh2-ém- > *φ (w)am- > fam-. I believe that the derivation is essentially correct, but I part with Vine on some details. First of all, I start from an HD1 paradigm (see below), which is not problematic for his views, for the HD1 middle stem would also provide the required * hh2 -ém-. In my opinion, we must be dealing with secondary metathesis (probably by analogy to the position of the laryngeal in the weak stem * huh2-m-, where the laryngeal metathesized before contiguous taustsyllabic semi-vowel and a consonant). Thus, in Latin famēs we find an m-stem that can be connected to the Germanic forms. This provides us with the means to explain the Germanic forms of the word for ‘palate, gums’. As discussed above, the Germanic forms show a remarkable variety in their stem forms and the attestations provide no less than five different stem forms: * ōm-, * a m-, * ūm-, *gumm-, and *guim-/guem-. We can unite the forms by first assuming an HD1 m-stem

paradigm:

<!-- page 129 -->

```text
NOM.SG * héh2 -m
ACC.SG * hh2 - m-m
LOC.SG * hh2 -ém-i
GEN.SG * huh2-m-ós
```

This paradigm can explain both the Latin and Germanic forms. For Latin we require the weak stem * hh2 -ém-, found in both the ACC.SG and LOC.SG. Furthermore, this provides us with a weak stem * huh2-m-, which formed the basis of analogical repositioning of the laryngeal in the middle stem * hh2 -ém- → * huh2-ém- > *φ (w)am- > fam-. In Germanic, the strong stem * heh2 -m yielded * m- with original root accentuation, where *ō < *ō < *eh2u by Mahlow’s Law of long diphthong monophthongization. The form * a m-' possibly continues the strong stem as well, but has secondary accentuation on the derivational suffix (on which see below). This form can best be explained from a mn-stem, however, as discussed below. The peculiar stem *guim- can be explained from the middle stem * hh2 -ém- directly if we assume loss of laryngeal in this environment. The form would then have a parallel in the stem of Latin fam-ēs. It is probable that the form * m- shows raising from the locative * hh2 -ém-i > PG + -im-i. On the other hand, the OHG forms may indeed be a mutated, monophthongized vowel. In my opinion, however, this is hardly plausible for a case form other than the dative in the singular. As I argued in §3.1.3 on p.62, OHG mutation first occurs around 1000 and since the earliest form guimo comes from the early 11th century guimo can in principle still represent an unmutated /guim-/. Furthermore, the form occurs within the first years in which the mutation is in effect, which makes it hardly plausbile to have already been generalized throughout the paradigm. I therefore propose to take the diphthong in guimo at face value and interpret the form as /guim-/. The four distinct Germanic forms can now be explained from a single HD1 m-stem paradigm. However, the derivation of the OHG form guimo from the middle stem * hh2 -ém- requires the loss of laryngeal in the same environment as the weak stem of the words for ‘fire’ and ‘sun’. The derivation from an HD1 paradigm that I propose here is the only natural way to account for this particular sound sequence without resorting to ad hoc developments. This form therefore provides probative evidence for the law I posited in §6.2, p.125. The Germanic stems for ‘palate, gum’ were n-stems in all dialects (save in ON gómr, which must be an innovation for it is an n-stem elsewhere in the Nordic language, cf. OSw. ōm, ODan. ōmæ, etc.). It is unclear how the break-down of the m-stem and its transfer to the n-stems proceeded exactly, but it is possible to explain the variation * ūm-: *gumm- from an n-stem derivative. First of all, * ūm- must have retained the accent on the root, for otherwise it would have been shortened by Dybo’s Law of pretonic shortening. It is quite possible that the original m-stem became restructured into an mn-stem (cf. Vine). Since the mn-stems were often AD, the new, restructured paradigm can be reconstructed anachronistically as follows:

```text
NOM.SG * héh2 -mōn > + mōn
ACC.SG * h(e)h2 -món-m > + ū man-un or +ga mán-un
GEN.SG * huh2-mn-ós > + ū mn-as > +gumm-as
DAT.SG * huh2-mn- > + ū mn-a > +gumm-a
```

<!-- page 130 -->

The weak stem form would then have become *gumm- by the assimilation of *-mn- > *-mmwith subsequent vowel shortening by Osthoff’s Law before resonant plus consonant. The presence of Osthoff’s Law in this form shows that the form must be considerably old. If we reconstruct an AD middle stem * heh2 -món-m we can obtain the variant * a m- naturally as well from the pretonic shortening of *au-' < *ō -' by Dybo’s Law before Mahlow’s Law could affect the long diphthong, i.e.: * heh2 m-' > * ō m-' > * a m-. However, the archaic form guimo cannot be derived from the mn-stem and must go back to the old m-stem.

## §6.4 THE WORD FOR ‘POLE, PROP’

The development of *eh2 -' > *a - in * heh2 m-' > * a m- finds a parallel in the development of ON staurr ‘pole’. The word has an exact cognate in Gr. σταυρός ‘pole’ < *steh2 s. For this attractive derivation to work in Germanic, Dybo’s Law has to affect the sequence *-eh2u- before Mahlow’s Law could have affected it and eliminated its semi-vowel (I return to the relative chronology of sound changes in §7.2.4 on p.138). It is striking that the accent in Gr. σταυρός is on the suffix, which can then also account for the development of *-eh2u-' > PG *-a - by Dybo’s Law, or: *steh2 ró- > *stō rá > *sta -. It is likely that ON stórr, OFri. stō, OS stō, OHG stuori, all: ‘great, strong, heavy’ < *stō - are cognate. Semantically, the connection is possible if the meaning of ‘pole, prop’ was used ethically, using the supporting strength of the pole metaphorically and developing from there. If so, the form *stō - would go back to *stéh2. This word is generally connected to *stū - in e.g. OHG stū ‘strong, great’, MLG stur(e) ‘harsh, dismissive’, WFri. stoer, stoersk ‘surly; sturdy, robust‘, which would present a zero-grade *stúh2r-. A PG *st - is also attested, which in principle may go back to a middle stem *sth2 ér- > *st - with laryngeal loss and subsequent metathesis to *st -. The form *st - is found in several derivations, e.g. *st rō (cf. OFri. st ū, OHG stiura ‘rudder, prop, pole’), *st - a- (in OS stiur-, MLG stiur, OE stēo - (me. stē ), OFri. st ū, -st ō (WFri. stjoer), ON stýri, all meaning ‘rudder’. The semantic development from ‘pole’ involved 1) an ethical dimension (both positive as ‘strong, etc.’ and negative as ‘robust > harsh’, etc.), and 2) the use of this word for pole for a rudder. The forms *stō -, *stū -, and *st - (if from older *st -) can be incorporated into a single HD1 paradigm *stéh2 - ‘something standing/that stands’ > ‘pole’ (cf. NIL:639 on the root *steh2- and its enlargement *steh2 -):

```text
PIE PG transpose PG deduction
NOM.SG *stéh2 - > +st *stō -
ACC.SG *sth2 -ér-m > +st ér- *st r-
GEN.SG *stuh2r-ós > +stur-' *stū -
```

The transpose of the strong stem *stéh2 - is PG +stōr, which corresponds directly to the stem *stō - reconstructed above. The middle stem *sth2 -ér- would yield the transpose +st ér-,

<!-- page 131 -->

which may very well have been normalized to the phonotactically more regular stem *ste r-. The weak stem *stuh2r-ós would yield +stur-' with pretonic shortening from Dybo’s Law, but the form deduced from the attested forms has a long vowel: *stū -. The root accent of the strong stem may have been leveled so that at one point there may even have been a paradigm with level stress on the root: *st /*sté r-/*stū r-. In any way, the variation of forms *sta r-, *stōr, *ste r-, *stū - conspicuously mirrors much of the variation in the forms * a m-, * ōm, *guim-/guem-, * ūm- (also *gumm-) and can be explained in a very similar fashion. It is remarkable that in Skt. we find the adjectives sth a aḥ ‘firm, standing’ < *steh2- er-o-, leveling strong R(e) and weak S(e), besides sthū aḥ ‘big, rough, fat’ (which developed a more or less similar ethical dimension as in Germanic), reflecting the weak stem *stuh2r- with R(∅)-E(∅), cf. also Lith. stûrs ‘tenacious’. The gradation patterns of these forms alone corroborate an original HD1 paradigm. A third form: stha aḥ ‘big, strong, rough, fat’, must be related but would (mechanically) go back to *ste h2r-o- with a metathesized laryngeal. It is quite possible that stha aḥ was formed from sthū aḥ ‘big, strong, rough, fat’, or rather from an earlier form IIr. *stuHr- with the laryngeal still present. That is, after *stuHr- ‘big, strong, rough, fat’ was semantically separated from what would become sth a aḥ ‘firm, standing’, a new ‘strong stem’ was created: *stuHra- → *sta Hra-. This can explain both the semantic identity of sthū aḥ ‘big, strong, rough, fat’ and stha aḥ ‘big, strong, rough, fat’ as well as the unexpected position of the laryngeal in stha aḥ. From these deliberations we can conclude that both the Germanic and Indic forms point to an original HD1 paradigm.

## §6.5 ON THE RELATIVE CHRONOLOGY OF SOME RELEVANT SOUND CHANGES

Nevertheless, the relative chronology that I propose of Dybo’s Law preceding Mahlow’s Law is not uncontroversial. In fact, Guus Kroonen (2012) has recently argued for a different relative chronology in a presentation at the 14th Fachtagung of the Indogermanische Gesellschaft, at the University of Copenhagen, on the 17th of December. A probative form for Kroonen’s scenario is offered by the word for ‘egg’, reconstructed as *ō m. The Germanic languages point to a PG form *a a- (ON egg, OS ei, OHG ei, OE æg, etc., all meaning ‘egg’), which appears to have formed s-stems in most languages. ON egg [n., o-st.] is certainly not an s-stem and goes back to PG *a an. The ON form corresponds directly to IE cognate neuter ostems (e.g. Gr. ᾠὀν, Lat. ovum ‘egg’), which must be old, and therefore derive directly from earlier *ō m, whereas the other languages innovated.69 Providing a very elegant and cogent account, Kroonen posits the following relative chronology: 69 The formation of an s-stem is usually taken to be in analogy to a number of other s-stem ‘farm words’ such as ‘lamb’, ‘calf’, etc., thus e.g. Philippa (2003-2009).

<!-- page 132 -->

```text
1) MAHLOW’S LAW: *ō m > *ō óm
2) DYBO’S LAW: *ō óm > *o óm
```

3) HOLTZMANN’S LAW: *o óm > *o óm The sequence of sound laws can not only account very nicely for this word, but Kroonen puts this and other words in a much wider perspective and relative chronology (most of which is not useful to our discussion, however). However, I do not believe that Mahlow’s Law could have preceded Dybo’s Law. The reason for this is that *-eh2(3) - did not always yield *-ō-, but *-a - when the accent did not fall on the diphthong, but succeeded it, cf. the following two

```text
examples:
*-a - < *-eh2 -' *-ō- < *-éh2 -
1. * a m- < * heh2 m-' * ōm- < * héh2 m-
2. *sta - < *steh2 -' *stō - < *stéh2 -
```

The existence of * a m- as well as * ōm- and *sta - as well as *stō - can in my opinion hardly be explained otherwise. The environment of the shortening of *-eh23 - > *-ō - > *-a - is evidently before a resonant plus accented vowel, which is the exact same environment as Dybo’s Law. This militates against the working of Mahlow’s Law before Dybo’s Law and is a serious problem for the derivation for the word for ‘egg’ as proposed by Kroonen. Furthermore, the reconstruction of the word for ‘egg’ as *ō m (or *h2ō m) is not without its problems. Zair (2011) has recently argued that *h2ō m is a h -derivation from the word for ‘bird’ *h2 -i-. However, a ddhi-derivation with *ō, so that *h2 i- ‘bird’ → *h2ō -, is entirely unparalleled in PIE and the environment for lengthening is lacking (on which see §1.2 p.24). Therefore this etymological connection is best left open. If Müller (2007:213f.) is correct, then the Greek forms such as ὤϊον and (Ionic and Attic) ᾠὀν point to *ō o- [n.] with loss of the glides *- - and *- -. Alternatively, Müller posits the following relative chronology for this word (whatever the exact phonetic nature of the initial phonemes of the word may be, I simply follow Kroonen’s non-commital reconstruction without

```text
laryngeal *#ō-):70
1) MAHLOW’S LAW: *ō o- > *ō o- (with ‘irreversible’ Sievers’ Law)
2) OSTHOFF’S LAW: *ō o- > *o o-
```

For Müller’s derivation to work, the Sievers’ Law form *ō o- had to retain the sequence *- - and become *- - between vowels after Mahlow’s Law affected the diphthong. However, it is known that Sievers’ Law actually was quite flexible and depended on the length of the preceding syllable so that the change *ō o- > *ō o- is ad hoc and unsubstantiated, and we would rather expect *ō o- > *ō o- instead. I therefore reject Müller’s proposal as well. As suggested by Müller (2007:214), the PIE word for ‘egg’ may have had a suffix *-ih2o-. Müller’s suggestion means that the reconstruct *ō o- should be amended to *ō h2o-. This reconstruction is all the more fortuitous, because we can now explain the Germanic geminate * from *ih2 regularly by Müller’s Law (on which see p.99 above), even though 70 Note that Müller does not himself refer to the first law as Mahlow’s Law.

<!-- page 133 -->

Müller (2007:213) himself is skeptical of resonant gemination by laryngeal in this particular form. Nevertheless, the laryngeal can explain both the development of the Greek forms as well as the geminate in Germanic by resonant gemination (i.e. by Müller’s Law). That is, a proto-form *ō h2o- in principle yields the required results in Greek and Germanic. A minor issue is the stress placement, for Müller’s Law requires that the accent is on the first syllable, but since we do not understand the derivation itself this may not trouble us too much. In fact, the Germanic material would best be interpretable as having initial accent (although this cannot be counted as evidence for root accent, for this would be circular reasoning), but this may easily have been secondary. To account for the Germanic development of the word *a a- I start from a deduced form * h2o- and I propose the following derivation instead:

```text
1) MAHLOW’S LAW: *ō h2o- > *ō h2o-
2) MÜLLER’S LAW: *ō h2o- > *ō o-
3) OSTHOFF’S LAW: *ō o- > *o o-
```

The first stage involves the development of *ō > *ō in open syllable. Then, in the following stage Müller’s Law affected the geminate via *ō h2o- > *ō o-. Note that Kroonen does not offer a specific formulation for Holtzmann’s Law or the gemination in the word for ‘egg’71. However, the proposal of Müller’s Law has the advantage of being well-defined, falsifiable, and constraining the possibilities of our reconstructions (even though some problems remain). Finally, the last stage involves the shortening of long *ō to short *o, yielding virtual *o o-, i.e. the required PG form *a a-. Kroonen adduces the form ON naust ‘boathouse’, originally a compound *neh2u-sth2ó- (cf. Harðarson, 2001:28), to show that Osthoff’s Law preceded Mahlow’s Law. However, Mahlow’s Law would not affect the diphthong in *neh2u-sth2-ó- > *nō sta- since the syllable is closed and Mahlow’s Law only works in open syllables according to Kroonen’s own formulation of Mahlow’s Law (cf. my formulation of Mahlow’s Law in the list of sound laws in §7.2.4 on p.138 below). This argument for a relative chronology must be rejected. In fact, ON naust can be derived readily from my proposed relative chronology as well in which Osthoff’s Law occurred later than Mahlow’s Law. In the following schema I have given my tentative proposal for a relative chronology, illustrated with the words for * h2o- ‘egg’, *stō - ‘pole, prop’ < *steh2- -ó-, *st o- ‘pole, prop’ < *stéh2- -o-, *nō sth2ó‘boathouse’ < *neh2-u-sth2-ó-, *nō s ‘ship’ < *neh2-u-s (cf. ON nór, Lat. navis, Gr. ναῦς, Skt. náus, etc. ‘ship’) (all forms are given with *ō and I disregard all other sound laws, exacept finally in the resulting PG forms where *o > *a is applied and NOM.SG *-z replaces *-s): 71 Kroonen refers to the gemination as Holtzmann’s Law, whereas I would choose to explain gemination as part of Müller’s Law and the Verschärfung or fortition of geminates in Norse and Gothic specifically as Holtzmann’s Law. Note that Kroonen leaves the details of the conditions of Holtzmann’s Law/gemination unspecified, although matters of brevity in his presentation may certainly have been a factor.

<!-- page 134 -->

```text
* h2o- *stō - *st o- *nō sth2ó- *nō s
1) DYBO’S LAW: " *sta - " " "
2) MAHLOW’S LAW: * h2o- " *st o- " *nōs
3) MÜLLER’S LAW: * o- " " " "
4) OSTHOFF’S LAW: *o o- " " *no sth2ó- "
PG reflex: *a a- *sta a- *st a- *na sta- *nōz
```

This tentative relative chronology works for all the words involved and the ones discussed in the previous paragraphs and has the advantage of being able to explain all the variants of *sta r-, *stōr, *ste r-, *stū - as well as * a m-, * ōm-, *guem-, * ūm- (also: *gumm-). In the preceding discussion I argued for a law of laryngeal loss and a specific relative chronology of sound changes. With these means a number of likely cognate forms can be connected, whereas they are otherwise difficult to connect. The above deliberations thus have strong explanatory power. The law of laryngeal loss occurs in five forms that are quire reliable: the weak stems *ph2 én > +fēn- ‘fire’, *sh2 én- > +sēn- ‘sun’, and the middle stems * hh2 -ém- > + m- ‘gums, palate’ and *sth2- -m > +st er- ‘pole’, as well as in the form *bh h1 -én-, which was remodeled. It is interesting to find that the pattern *#CH é- had to outcomes in Germanic: 1. *#CH é- > *#C é- and was reinterpreted as *#C -é-and remodeled as *#C C-é- in

accordance to minimal root constraints;

2. *#CH é- > *#C é- > *#C i- (or *#CH í- > *#C í-) by raising before a high vowel

and was retained.

This pattern is in itself conspicuous and requires an explanation. The pattern can be readily explained in my account. Nevertheless, the number of forms that support laryngeal loss in the sequence *#CH é- (perhaps also *#CH í-) is admittedly small. The phonetic environment, too, is quite hard to explain. It is possible that the environment is somewhat broader than I have formulated. Perhaps the loss of laryngeal occurred in *#CH I-, where I = /e, ē, i, ī/, or perhaps even *# I-, which renders the phonetic justification much less problematic.72 The precise extent of the phonetic environment has to be borne out of further research, however. I conclude by tentatively positing the law of laryngeal loss in a broader environment as follows:

1. LAW OF LARYNGEAL LOSS. – A laryngeal is lost after a word-initial consonant and before a contiguous semivowel plus a following accented high front vowel Í /e, ē, i, ī/, so that:

```text
*H > ∅ | #C__UÍ-
```

72 It is for instance not impossible that Go. fijan ‘hate’, which goes back to PG *f an- instead of *fī an-, from a present ph1i-()e- (cf. LIV2:459f., which has to assume a Germanic innovation), which may show the loss of laryngeal in the sequence *Ch1 -. This is admittedly highly speculative at this point, however.

<!-- page 135 -->

# Chapter 7. CONCLUSION

## §7.1 SUMMARY AND CONCLUSION

In this thesis I have discussed the remnants of heteroclite r/n-stems in Germanic. Germanic continues a number of interesting reflexes of heteroclite nouns that sometimes offer a unique perspective on the Indo-European material. What is of particular interest is the partially parallel development of four heteroclite words: *péh2 /*ph2uén- ‘fire’, *séh2 l /*sh2uén- ‘sun’, *bhréh1 /*bhruh1én- ‘well, source’, and *sk h1- /*skuh1-én‘shelter, shed’. This development in itself suggests that the forms constituted a class unto themselves in Germanic. This class probably disintegrated not long before the break off of Gothic. Gothic is exceptional among the Germanic languages for continuing two living heteroclites: the word fon/fun-in- ‘fire’ and the word for ‘sun’ sauïl/sunn-. Especially the word for ‘sun’ sauïl/sunn- is unique and the only reflex among the Indo-European languages that truly continues a heteroclite paradigm. In short, the development of the four heteroclites can be summarized in the following

```text
schema:
STAGE I STAGE II STAGE III
*bhruh1én- → *brūn- → *brun-en- → *brunn-(e)n-
*sh2uén- > +sēn- → *sun-en- → *sunn-(e)n-
*ph2uén- > +fēn- → *fun-en-
*skuh1-én- > +skuen-
```

The parallel development from stage I to II shows the reinterpretation of heteroclite weak stems as HD2 n-stems. In WG, however, the paradigm ‘fire’ was leveled to *f r-. Both the forms *f r- and +skuen- generalized a raised vowel from the dative, so that the resulatant forms *f ir- and *skuin- could not be interpreted as n-stems. In Stage II, the collective weak stem was inserted to satisfy Germanic morpheme structure constraints, because the structure *Cnu-en- was not allowed. The new structure *Cnun-en- developed an early n-stem with geminate from *brunn-en- and *sunn-(e)n- from the HD2 paradigm. As said, the parallel development suggests that the forms constituted a class in later Germanic. A hypothetical feature of this derivation is that the development relies on the law of laryngeal loss, where *H > ∅ in the sequence *#CH é-. It is interesting to find that the sequence *#CH é- had two distinct outcomes in Germanic, namely:

<!-- page 136 -->

1. *#CH é- > *#C é- and was subsequently reinterpreted as *#C -é-and remodeled as

*#C C-é- in accordance to the Germanic minimal root constraints;

2. *#CH é- > *#C é- > *#C i- (or *#CH í- > *#C í-) by raising before a high vowel

and this structure was retained.

For the first development the following relatively secure forms can be adduced: 1. PG *fun-en- < weak stem *ph2uén- (cf. ON funi, Go. fun-in-) 2. PG *sunn- < *sh2uén- (cf. ON sunna, Go. sunna, etc.) 3. PG *brunn- < *bh h2 n- (cf. Go. brunna, etc.) For the second development, the following relatively secure forms can be deduced (assuming raising of *e > *i in Germanic): 1. PG *fuir < weak stem *ph2uén- (with leveling of the strong suffix *-r, cf. OHG

<uugir>, etc.)

2. PG *suin- < weak stem *sh2uén- (cf. ON syn or sýn) 3. PG *guim- < HD weak stem * hh2 -ém- (cf. OHG guimo, giumo ‘palate’) 4. (PG *skuin- < *skuh1-én-, which did not have the environment *#CH é-, but

```text
which only retained the structure *#C é- with a raised vowel as did the other
heteroclites in Germanic)
```

I conclude this thesis by providing an overview of relevant PIE gradation types and laws of vowel lengthening, a broad formulation of the law of laryngeal loss, and a list of formulations of relevant Germanic sound laws ordered in a relative chronology.

<!-- page 137 -->

## §7.2 OVERVIEW OF GRADATION TYPES AND SOUND LAWS

### §7.2.1 SURVEYABLE REPRESENTATION OF PIE GRADATION TYPES

```text
PD1 = |é  ∅  ∅|        PD2 = |ó  ∅|          AD  = |é  ṓ|
      |∅  é  ∅|              |∅  é  ∅|             |∅  ó  ∅|
                                                     |∅  ∅  ó|

HD1 = |é  ∅  ∅|        HD2 = |∅  ḗ|          HD3 = |∅  ṓ|
      |∅  é  ∅|              |∅  é  ∅|              |∅  ∅  ó|
      |∅  ∅  ó|              |∅  ∅  ó|
```

### §7.2.2 FORMULATION OF PIE SOUND LAWS

1. LAW OF FINAL LENGTHENING: Short vowels before contiguous resonant at word end are lengthened, so that *-VR# becomes *-V̄R#, where R = /r, l, m, n, u̯, i̯/ or:

```text
*V > *V̄ | __R#
```

2. LAW OF MONOSYLLABIC LENGTHENING. – Monosyllables lengthen a short vowel when followed by either a contiguous plosive P or resonant R, but not a fricative *H or *s, plus another voiceless dental T = */s, t/ (including the dental clusters /st, ts/), or:

```text
*-V- > *-V̄- | #Cn__{P/R}T#
```

### §7.2.3 LAW OF LARYNGEAL LOSS

1. LAW OF LARYNGEAL LOSS. – A laryngeal is lost after a word-initial consonant and before a contiguous semivowel plus a following accented high front vowel Í /e, ē, i, ī/, so that:

```text
*H > ∅ | #C__UÍ-
```

<!-- page 138 -->

### §7.2.4 THE FORMULATION AND RELATIVE CHRONOLOGY OF SOME GERMANIC SOUND LAWS

1. DYBO’S LAW. – A long vowel *V̄, or the long vowel in a long diphthong, is shortened to *V, or the corresponding short diphthong, if it is followed by a contiguous resonant R, where R = /u̯, i̯, r, l, n, m/, followed by a vowel bearing stress V́, so that:

```text
*V̄ > V | __(U)RV́
```

2. MAHLOW’S LAW.73 – A long diphthong *ōu in a Germanic root syllable becomes *ō if it is followed by a contiguous single consonant either followed by a vowel or at a word’s end, so that:

```text
             V
*ōu > *ō | __C
             #
```

3. MÜLLER’S LAW. – A resonant R is geminated to RR in a Germanic root syllable if it is followed by a contiguous laryngeal plus an unstressed vowel and preceded by an accented vowel, so that:

```text
*-V́RHV- > *-V́RRV-
```

4. OSTHOFF’S LAW. – A long vowel in a Germanic root syllable is shortened to V if it is followed by a contiguous resonant R, where R = /u̯, i̯, r, l, n, m/, plus any other consonant C, so that:

```text
*V̄ > V | __RC
```

73 Note that Mahlow’s Law does not work strictly in open syllables, but also occurs in monosyllables like Go. *fon*, which derives from *fō < *fōu < *peh2u*, and *sōl < *sōul < *seh2ul*. It seems to me that therefore the conditions of Mahlow’s Law need to be slightly altered as presented here.

<!-- page 139 -->

Recommended third pass only if needed for publication/citation: restore italics/typographic distinctions and manually proofread every reconstructed form against page images.

Second-pass audit note (2026-04-28):
- Targeted visual verification was applied to the phonemic cover-symbol table, the §0.4 notation passage, the major gradation matrices in ch. 1 and ch. 7, key heteroclite paradigm tables, the §6.2 laryngeal-loss law, and visibly damaged bibliography entries.
